Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
The focus of this thesis is on forecasting the water production of gas wells at a gas stor­
age facility. This knowledge may assist in optimising the production patterns at a facility
operated by Gas Storage Denmark. Data­driven models were implemented for solving
the forecasting task using 11 and a half years of measurements from 14 gas wells.
The models ARIMA, K`BK and LSTM were developed for each individual well. The
predictions from the ARIMA and K`BK models merely consisted of past observations
with some decay, closely resembling forecasts of a naïve random walk model. The LSTM
models showed somewhat promising forecasting results for two of the wells. Further
development and tuning should be made to improve the quality of the forecasts.
Analysing the results of the models, it became apparent that the main issue related to the
poor performance of the models was caused by data set shifts since the water production
patterns change over time. The varying water production was taken into consideration
when making the data partitions, but this approach was seen to be insufficient given the
severity of the problem.
Keywords: natural gas storage, water production forecasting, multivariate time series,
ARIMA, K`BK, LSTM.
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning

Notation and terminology
In this thesis, we use the following notation. Uppercase bold letters for matrices A, bold
lowercase letters for vectors a and lowercase letters for scalars a. The Hadamard or
element­wise product is denoted by d. Vectors are assumed to be column vectors.
A stochastic process is denoted with capital letters Yt and time series are denoted with
lower case letters yt.
The term gas production is used interchangeably with gas extraction.

1
Introduction
The main focus of this thesis is time series forecasting on a multivariate data set supplied
by the company Gas Storage Denmark A/S. Specifically, the objective is to accurately
forecast the variable ’water production rate’ seven days into the future. In this chapter, an
overview of the gas storage facility in Stenlille and its operational procedures is provided.
This constitutes background knowledge for the forecasting problem at hand.
1.1
Gas Storage Denmark
Natural gas is used as an energy source in Denmark and the gas is distributed to con­
sumers throughout the country via the national gas transmission network. The gas enters
the transmission system from Germany and from gas fields in the North Sea. There are
two gas storage facilities connected to the transmission network in Denmark, one in Lille
Torup and one in Stenlille, both of which are operated by Gas Storage Denmark (GSD).
This project is concerned with the gas storage facility in Stenlille.
The motivation for the construction and operation of these storage facilities is both to meet
the higher demand for gas during winter and to provide an emergency reserve of gas in
case of problems regarding the gas supply. The operation of a storage facility is also
made financially viable by the seasonal fluctuations in demand as natural gas is generally
produced at a steady rate throughout the year. Therefore, gas distribution companies
(shippers) rent storage space at GSD to store gas bought in summer when demand and
prices are low. The shippers then sell the gas in winter when prices are higher. For
this reason, the facility in Stenlille is operated in a yearly seasonal pattern where gas is
injected into the facility in summer and extracted from the facility in winter.
Figure 1.1: Cross section of the underground 1500 m below Stenlille where gas is stored.
Please note the difference in scale between the axes. From Stade et al. 2020, [70].
The facility in Stenlille utilises natural sandstone formations to store gas around 1500 m
below ground level. Water naturally resides in the sandstone and is displaced by the
injected gas. A cross­section of the storage area is shown in Figure 1.1 with the gas­filled
sandstone highlighted in red and the water­filled sandstone highlighted in blue. The clay
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
1
==PAGE:12==
Chapter 1. Introduction
layers are shown in green behave as lids for the gas, which prevents the gas from flowing
vertically up. In the illustration, Figure 1.1, four separate gas storage reservoirs seem to
be visible, however, the sealing between the tree upper reservoirs allows for some vertical
movement between them. For this reason, they are regarded as one gas storage reservoir
called Zone 1­3. The bottom reservoir is completely disconnected from Zone 1­3, and this
gas storage reservoir is called Zone 5. The reservoir mechanisms are explained further
in Section 3.1.
Wells are used to inject and extract gas from the underground reservoirs. There are a total
of 14 operational wells connected to the gas storage reservoirs, and each operational well
only has perforations to one of the two reservoirs. Additionally, there are six wells used for
observational purposes and one of these wells, ST­6, is used to re­inject extracted water.
The extracted water is re­injected as it cannot easily be disposed of otherwise and the
process can be expensive. The wells are generally numbered in order of completion date
and they are listed in Table 1.2 along with their usage. It is the 14 operative wells spread
across Zone 1­3 and Zone 5 that are of interest in this project.
Table 1.2: The table lists the wells at Stenlille and whether they are used for observational
purposes or have perforations to one of the two zones, Stade et al. 2020, [70].

1.1.1
Operation of gas plant
During summer, when gas is injected into the reservoirs, water is displaced downwards.
Conversely, the water level rises in the reservoirs during the extraction season when gas
is extracted, which eventually leads to the wells discharging water along with the gas. The
mechanism underlying the rising water level around the wells’ perforations is expected to
be strongly affected by water coning, which is described in more detail in Section 3.1.1.
This phenomenon makes it less than trivial to predict when the wells start producing water
and only approximate analytical models for water coning exits.
The facility as a whole has an upper limit for the amount of water it can handle, as ST­6
can re­inject water at a maximum rate of 12 m3/h. Therefore, if a well starts to produce
significant amounts of water, it might be taken out of service. For the reasons mentioned
above, forecasting the water production would be beneficial. The extraction rate of a well
could then be adjusted accordingly to prevent the effects of a rising water level, which will
allow for a better utilisation of the storage facility.
Numerical approaches where water coning is modelled using reservoir data are generally
considered more accurate than the analytical solutions. This is due to reliance on as­
sumptions of the latter and the heterogeneous nature of the reservoirs, (Okon et al. 2017,
[48]). The above provides the motivation for this project, which is to predict the water
production as described further in Section 1.2.
1.1.2
Current methods
GSD is required to always have a minimum amount of gas available such that they are
able to supply the demand for gas in Denmark for a period of time in case of a shortage of
supplies from other sources. Therefore, GSD uses a simple curve, which estimates the
2
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:13==
1.1. Gas Storage Denmark
Figure 1.3: Example of the curve used by GSD to estimate the extraction rate as a function
of cumulative extraction. From [33].
total production capacity of the facility given how much gas has been extracted during a
production season. This is then supplied to the Transmission System Operator, Energinet
every year. In the method, which is exemplified in Figure 1.3, the facility as a whole is as­
sumed to able to produce at a given capacity until the cumulative gas extraction exceeds
a threshold after which the extraction rate drops linearly. The specifics of how this curve
was made are not known but the levels are adjusted slightly every year by making cor­
rections based on the estimated quality of the gas among others. This method estimates
the production for a whole season, and cannot be used for short­term forecasting.
Figure 1.4: The plots show the cumulative water production as a function of the cumulative
gas production. From Haspang 2020, [24].
On the scale of individual wells, plotting the cumulative water production as a function of
the cumulative gas production for each season may be used to estimate when an increase
in the water production is going to happen. This is shown in Figure 1.4 for two of the wells
which are seen to follow different trends. The crosses may then be estimated using a
criterion such as the water gas ratio. This method still remains experimental.
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
3
==PAGE:14==
Chapter 1. Introduction
In relation to daily operations, much is performed on an ad­hoc basis where operational
patterns from past seasons are largely repeated with adjustments based on live data.
Generally, GSD aims at keeping the extraction rates low, while still meeting demand, in
order to avoid water coning and sand production though the latter is mostly considered
unimportant. Injection rates are also kept low both to avoid pushing gas over the edge of
the reservoir during the injection season and in order to keep the pressure below the crit­
ical pressure of the overburden, which avoids damage to the top seal. During extraction,
a well is turned off if it starts producing too much water, which is also based on feed­
back from live data and experience relating to how quickly water production increases for
the individual well. Apart from adjusting production, the live data also helps in avoiding
potential accidents and damage to the equipment, (Haspang 2020, [24]).
1.2
Project aim and overview
As described in this chapter, GSD does at the moment not have any methods for accu­
rately predicting the water production of the wells. Therefore, the aim of this project is to
develop a data­driven method for forecasting the water production of the wells at Stenlille.
Due to the time constraints of the project, we focus on producing forecasts up to one week
ahead. Such a method would serve as a tool in weakly planning and it could be used to
optimise the production.
The data­driven approach was chosen because of the untapped potential of the data, and
the promising results from similar studies, which are described in Chapter 2. The theory
of water coning is outlined in Chapter 3 along with the theory behind the models explored
in this thesis. Water coning has for a long time been assumed to be the main mechanism
behind the water production, but a recent experiment at Stenlille suggests that this might
not be the case. Here, a well was opened 1.5 months after water breakthrough where it
immediately began to produce water at the same rate as before the period of inactivity.
The data­driven approach applied in this project should, however, be feasible regardless
of which exact mechanisms are responsible for the water production.
The data for this task consists of measurements of gas injection, gas extraction, temper­
ature, pressure and water production for each well along with several derived variables
based on the measured variables. The measurements were made from 2009 to 2021
and are stored as five­minute samples, (Stade et al. 2020, [70]). The data includes faulty
measurements so the data set was cleaned prior to modelling as described in Chapter 4.
The two main models explored in this project are the ARIMA model and a neural network
based on the LSTM block. Additionally, a multivariate extension of the ARIMA model
was considered but only developed for a few of the time series since this approach was
deemed infeasible based on the early results. The implementations and developments
are described in Chapter 5 while their results are compared and contrasted in Chapter 6
followed by a conclusion and a suggestion for further works in Chapter 7.
4
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:15==
2
Related works
An overview of related works is provided in this chapter. This is limited to the ARIMA
models as well as neural networks since these are the two overall model frameworks
used in this thesis. A brief overview of additional methods and models relevant for time
series forecasting are included in Appendix B.
2.1
Use of ARIMA models in recent studies
ARIMA models are often used as baselines in recent studies on time series forecasting
where rigorous comparisons on univariate time series data show that the classical sta­
tistical models such as ARIMA still provide competitive forecasts, (Parmezan et al. 2019,
[51], Mallikarjuna et al. 2019, [47], Mahmoud et al. 2021, [46], Zhang et al. 2020, [86]).
In a comprehensive survey by Hewamalage et al. 2021, [28], a range of models including
ARIMA and different types of RNN­style architectures were compared on multiple data
sets with different characteristics. On some data sets, the ARIMA models provided the
most accurate forecasts while the RNN models were more accurate on others. It was
suggested that the reason for the underperformance of neural networks in relation to time
series modelling is that the univariate time series are often too short or have characteristics
that change over time. ARIMA models are in essence univariate models, and therefore,
neural networks may be superior for multivariate time series forecasting where they may
learn from correlated variables. This argument is also presented by Toubeau et al. 2019,
[79] who performed electric load forecasting based on multivariate data. Here, an LSTM
network is compared favourably to an ARIMA model.
Papacharalampous et al. 2019, [50] showed ARIMA models to be on par with neural net­
works for univariate time series forecasting. They argue that the best model is dependant,
not only on the data set, but also on the performance metric. In a study by Athiyarath et
al. 2020, [6], it is argued that if ARIMA models can be used to describe the underlying
process of a time series they provide useful long term forecasts. In this study, forecasting
was performed on three univariate data sets. An LSTM network was shown to provide
more accurate short term forecasts, whereas the ARIMA model was better at the mid­
and long­term forecasts.
Within the oil and gas industry, ARIMA models have been compared to LSTM models for
petroleum forecasting by Sagheer et al. 2019, [57]. The data set was univariate and in
this case, the LSTM network outperformed the ARIMA model. In Sheremetov et al. 2013,
[63], an ARIMA model provided less accurate forecasts than a neural network.
2.1.1
Vector ARMA (VARMA)
Possible ways of handling multivariate time series data within the ARIMA framework ex­
ist, but the main purpose and strength of the ARIMA model is univariate time series mod­
elling. The use of multivariate ARIMA models in the literature is limited. García Nieto
et al. 2018, [15] implemented the vectorised extension of the ARMA called VARMA. In­
terestingly, VARMA seems to do worse than ARIMA though this may be due to a lack of
feature selection.
2.2
Time series forecasting using neural networks
Deep learning models and recurrent neural networks in particular have been applied much
to the problem of stock market forecasting. Jiang 2020, [32] provides an overview of re­
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
5
==PAGE:16==
Chapter 2. Related works
cent works in this field where deep learning models are the most popular choice. Within
this category, RNN seems to be the preferred type of architecture where the Adam op­
timiser is used to tune the majority of the networks. RNNs are also the most popular
forecasting method in hydrology as described by Tiyasha et al. 2020, [77]. In general,
neural networks are observed to yield accurate short­term predictions whereas long­term
predictions are often less accurate than those provided by classical statistical models. It
is often reasoned that long­term predictions force the neural network model to extrapo­
late beyond what it has been exposed to in the training, which impairs the quality of the
forecasts.
Hewamalage et al. 2021, [28] describes how the recurrent neural networks generally have
trouble in modelling seasonality directly though they have been shown to be able to model
regular seasonal patterns on their own. One way of overcoming this is by deseasonali­
sation of the time series prior to modelling using for instance STL Decomposition. This
decomposes the time series into seasonal, trend and residual parts. The trend part is
found by approximately fitting a trend line and subtracting this from the time series. Simi­
larly, a seasonal part is modelled and subtracted from the time series, which may then be
added to the output from the neural network.
2.2.1
Long Short­term Memory (LSTM)
The original Long Short­Term Memory (LSTM) block was introduced by Hochreiter et al.
1997, [29] to deal with the gradient problems in the vanilla RNN. Here, the long­term
memory of the LSTM block was demonstrated in an experiment where the goal was to
predict the next symbol in a sequence. The LSTM was able to predict symbols using
information from up to 1000 lags ago whereas the vanilla RNN could not predict any
symbols when the input occurred more than 10 lags previously. In two experiments with
vectors, specific lags had to be either added or multiplied to produce the desired output of
the sequence. In these, the LSTM network was also successful, which showed its ability
to combine information from several previous lags. According to Hochreiter et al. 1997,
[29], this task had not been solved by other recurrent algorithms at the time.
In the original LSTM paper, the cell state is only updated by the input gate. This may cause
the internal cell state to grow unbounded, and therefore, the forget gate was introduced by
Gers 1999, [18]. In the original experiments performed by Hochreiter et al. 1997, [29], the
cell state was reset to zero after each input sequence. Therefore, the potential issue of
an unbounded cell state was never encountered. Gers 1999, [18] performed experiments
with continuous sequences that showed how the LSTM network with forget gate was able
to make successful predictions in cases where the original LSTM network failed due to
the unbounded cell state.
In the backpropagration of the LSTM block, the update on the cell state only depends on
the cell state itself which is the major improvement compared with the vanilla RNN. In
Greff et al. 2015, [21], different types of updates for the RNN were tried and it was found
that none were significantly better than the LSTM in general, though some produced an
improvement for specific applications.
Using a network architecture with stacked LSTM blocks and skip connections opens up
the possibility for training even deeper networks which have been shown to yield accu­
rate time series forecasts by Smyl 2020, [67] who also combined the recurrent neural
network with exponential smoothing methods. The overall architecture drew inspiration
from ResNet where convolutional blocks with skip connections are used to create a deep
network structure for image data.
6
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:17==
2.2. Time series forecasting using neural networks
Another way of combining LSTM blocks are the sequence­to­sequence (Seq2Seq) archi­
tecture where an input sequence is mapped to an output sequence using an encoder­
decoder structure. However, Hewamalage et al. 2021, [28] found that a stacked architec­
ture for RNN works better than a Seq2Seq structure for univariate time series forecasting.
The LSTM variant of the RNN has been shown to provide good performance on both
univariate and multivariate time series in multiple areas. Most interesting for the work in
this thesis, it has been used for forecasting production in the oil and gas industry, (Loh
et al. 2018, [44], Bao et al. 2020, [7], Liu et al. 2020, [41], Fan et al. 2021, [13], Liu et
al. 2019, [42], Heghedus et al. 2019, [27]). Moreover, it has been applied to weather
forecasts, (Karevan et al. 2020, [34]), and Yang et al. 2020, [84] applied LSTM networks
to tidal wave forecasting were it was found to be the superior method when compared with
ARIMA, SVR and CNN among others. Furthermore, LSTM has provided useful forecasts
within many other fields, (Thai­Nghe et al. 2020, [74], Salinas et al. 2020, [59], Thai­Nghe
et al. 2020, [75], Mahmoud et al. 2021, [46]).
LSTM­ARIMA
Several variants and hybrid models based on the LSTM block and to a lesser extent the
GRU block have also been employed in time series forecasting and one of these is the
ARIMA­LSTM where the residuals from the classical ARIMA model are used as input to
an RNN. This may also help the neural network in overcoming the difficulty of modelling
seasonality. This variant has also been applied to oil and gas production forecasting by
Fan et al. 2021, [13] where it was found to outperform pure models of either type.
2.2.2
GRU
The GRU block is a simpler version of the LSTM block which has two gates, update (zt)
and reset (rt), instead of three gates as the input and forget gate may be seen as being
combined into one. It also eliminates the cell state, however, the hidden state now fulfils
its role of improving the gradient flow in the backpropagation procedure. The equations
describing the updates in each iteration may be written as:



The simpler structure of the block compared with LSTM may cause it to require less data
to train, however, Hewamalage et al. 2021, [28] showed the LSTM unit to be better for
forecasting on univariate time series. Al­Shabandar et al. 2021, [62] used a GRU net­
work for petroleum production forecasting where it performed best compared with neural
networks using the LSTM block and the vanilla RNN.
2.2.3
CNN
Convolutional neural networks (CNN) have been applied to time series data with promis­
ing results. These type of networks have been widely used in computer vision where the
combination of convolutions and pooling operations have been shown to be very suc­
cessful in extracting useful features from images. In the case of time series analysis, they
have both been applied to spectrograms and unmodified time series, (Theodoridis 2020,
[76]).
CNN for feature extraction (CRNN)
Some studies including Yuan et al. 2021, [85] have proposed to use a hybrid of CNN and
RNN where the convolutional neural network makes up the first part of the hybrid network.
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
7
==PAGE:18==
Chapter 2. Related works
In this way, the CNN is used to extract features from the time series and hereby make a
summary of the data which may then be treated more easily by a recurrent neural network.
Lai et al. 2018, [38] showed that a CNN­LSTM hybrid performed best on multivariate time
series for both short and long term prediction compared with other models including AR,
SVR, GP and other neural networks. Abdullayeva et al. 2019, [2] used a CNN­RNN hybrid
for oil production forecasting where it compared favourably with pure networks of either
type.
2.2.4
TCN
A fairly recent variant of the CNN is the Temporal Convolutional Network (TCN) proposed
by Lea et al. 2016, [40]. They may be viewed as a unified approach to the hybrid CNN­
RNN models and are built using an encoder­decoder structure. They use what is known
as a causal convolution where elements at time step t are not convoluted with elements
at future time steps but only with previous time steps. While these might be very powerful,
they also seem to be more difficult to tune than other types of neural networks according
to Lara­Benitez et al. 2020, [39].
Wan et al. 2019, [81] used TCN on PM2.5 forecasting where it compared favourably with
LSTM­style networks. Yan et al. 2020, [83] used TCN in the realm of weather forecasting
where it was used for the prediction of ENSO with good results.
2.2.5
Attention mechanisms
Using attention mechanisms in neural networks provides a vector that summarises the
importance of each of the outputs. In an image, the vector would correspond to a grid
where each value encodes the importance of an image patch. In natural language pro­
cessing, the attention vector containing attention weights show which words in the input
are important for each word in the output. Attention­based RNN has been successfully
used for time series forecasts, (Shih et al. 2019, [65], Qin et al. 2017, [53], Liu et al. 2020,
[43]), but is mostly compared to other attention­based networks. In practice, the atten­
tion vector may be obtained from a feed forward neural network whose input is both the
hidden state of the RNN and the output of all the units that should be summarised by the
attention vector.
2.2.6
Pre­training of neural networks
Unsupervised learning can be used for pre­training the weights of a neural network in­
stead of randomly initialising the weights. In an encoder­decoder architecture, the input
is mapped to a subspace by the encoder and then reconstructed by the decoder. This is
done by Sagheer et al. 2019, [58] where an LSTM is used both as encoder and decoder.
Using the pre­training methods, they improve the accuracy of the forecasts, and further
argue that it works best for multivariate data sets which have correlated variables.
8
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:19==
3
Theory
In this chapter, an overview of the reservoir mechanics and the theory of water coning is
presented. This brief overview is intended as background knowledge for the modelling
task. Subsequently, the theory of the specific models selected for the task is outlined. This
outline includes the ARIMA and VARIMA models as well as the recurrent neural network
with a focus on the LSTM variant.
3.1
Overview of selected reservoir mechanics
At Stenlille, the gas is stored in the geological formation Gassum. The two reservoirs,
Zone 1­3 and Zone 5, can store gas as the sandstone in this formation contains small
cavities on the order of micrometers that are normally filled with water, (Stade et al. 2020,
[70]). The volume of these interconnected cavities or pores determines the storage ca­
pability of the rock and the ratio of this joined pore space to the total volume is known as
the effective porosity of the rock, (Ahmed 2019, [4]). At Stenlille, the porosity of the sand­
stone in Gassum is 20­28%. The gas is prevented from moving vertically upwards from
the reservoirs by the formation Fjerritslev, which makes up the cap rock of the reservoir,
(Stade et al. 2020, [70]).
(a) Gas immediately after injection.
(b) The injected gas has dispersed.
Figure 3.1: Visualisation of gas injection in a well. Please note that ’Water’ and ’Gas’ is
actually filled sandstone, which in Zone 5 consists of the layer ’5 Sand’. Also, the boundary
between the water­filled and gas­filled sandstone is irregular. As seen by the depths, the
dimensions of the illustration are vastly exaggerated. These depths are specific to the well
ST­20 and these are the vertical distances (TVD) with respect to mean sea level (MSL).
As the wells are generally not dug entirely vertically, these distances differ slightly from the
measured depths (MD), which are normally measured with respect to the kelly bushing
(KB) on the well. The plug back total depth (PBTD) is the depth to cement plug at the
bottom of the well. Adapted from Stade et al. 2020, [70].
When gas is injected into the reservoirs, the water in the cavities around the well’s per­
forations is displaced. The resulting structure may be pictured as an irregular bubble of
gas­filled sandstone surrounding the perforations as sketched in Figure 3.1a. Due to the
differences in density, the gas will eventually spread out across the gas storage reservoirs
such that a horizontal boundary between gas­filled sandstone and water­filled sandstone
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
9
==PAGE:20==
Chapter 3. Theory
is formed. This is pictured in Figure 3.1b. In reality, the boundary between gas and water
is a transitional area where capillary effects are expected to play a considerable role in
deforming it.
This horizontal region takes some time to form due to the finite permeability of the sand­
stone. Permeability is a measure of a rock’s ability and capacity to let fluids pass through
it. The influence of the horizontal permeability (kh) on the flow rate (q) of a horizontal linear
flow of an incompressible fluid is given by the equation known as the Darcy’s Law:
q = ´khA
µ
dp
dL,
(3.1)
where µ is the viscosity of the fluid, A is the area of the cross­section and dp
dL is the change
in pressure per length. However, as the sandstone is not homogeneous, its permeability
is also affected by the configuration of the interconnected cavities among others. More­
over, for the horizontal permeability kh in Equation (3.1), it is assumed that the rock is
fully saturated with a single fluid. The sandstone in the reservoir is typically saturated
with more than one fluid, so the effective permeability should be used instead. This is a
property associated with a particular fluid such as gas and the permeability to the phase
decreases with its saturation, (Ahmed 2019, [4]). In general, the permeability is more dif­
ficult to measure than porosity. For the sandstone at Stenlille, the permeability of gas is
approximately a few hundred millidarcys (mD). The time it takes for the gas to spread out
evenly after injection is generally not known but it is assumed to be on the scale of a few
weeks according to GSD. This estimate is based on a limited number of logs, (Haspang
2020, [24]).
A complication of gas extraction is the possible formation of hydrates. This occurs when
water and hydrocarbons combine to form an ice­like crystalline material, which may clog
equipment. The natural gas is saturated with water vapour due to the presence of water
in the reservoir. The solidification may occur when temperatures are low and pressures
moderate, (Archer et al. 1986, [5]). At Stenlille, these conditions are most probable during
winter when extraction from a well begins after a period of inactivity since the pipe are cold
in that situation. During operation, the wells are heated to around 45˝C by the gas and
the problem vanishes.
3.1.1
Water coning
During extraction from the reservoirs, the gas surrounding the wells’ perforations is drawn
out first which causes a cone of water to rise. The instance where the cone reaches the
wells’ openings is known as water breakthrough or simply breakthrough. This is illustrated
in Figure 3.2a. The phenomenon is known as water coning and it is observed in both
in oil and gas production, however, water coning as it occurs in oil production is more
prevalent in literature, (Okon et al. 2017, [48]). Broadly speaking, when the viscous forces
dominate over the gravitational ones, the water level immediately below the perforations
will rise. This may happen if the extraction rate is high and the permeability is sufficiently
low. Additionally, in a depleted reservoir where the height of the gas column is small, the
water­gas contact will be close to the perforations from the onset, meaning a smaller cone
will still lead to water production, (Ahmed 2019, [4]).
Accurate modelling of water coning in reservoirs is a complex task which is reviewed in
Okon et al. 2017, [48]. Both analytical descriptions and empirical correlations have been
investigated, but these have typically been developed for a system that is in a steady­state
due to a fixed extraction rate. This description is useful for a well, which only performs
extraction, but it does not reflect the situation at GSD’s facility accurately. Moreover, since
10
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:21==
3.1. Overview of selected reservoir mechanics
(a) Water coning during extraction.
(b) Water level after coning.
Figure 3.2: Schematic of water coning in a well with the depths of ST­20. The drawing on
the left shows how the pressure drop in the well during extraction tends to draw up water.
The arrows are an illustration of the flow, which is much more complex in practice. On
the right is the water level after it has had time to flatten. Adapted from Stade et al. 2020,
[70] and Okon et al. 2017, [48].
the literature have almost exclusively focused on extraction, many of these approaches
have been concerned with finding the critical production rate in a steady­state environment
where the extraction rate is fixed over long periods. The critical production rate is the
rate above which the water cone breaks into the well due to the pressure. Others have
estimated the breakthrough time, which is the time after which the water level has reached
the perforations of the well at a given production rate, (Ahmed 2019, [4]).
One of these estimates for the breakthrough time tBT is given by the Bournazel­Jeanson
Method, which is an analytical approach based on empirical findings, (Bournazel et al.
1971, [10]). Even though The Bournazel­Jeanson Method is developed for estimating the
breakthrough time in oil reservoirs, it provides an intuition for the factors influencing water
coning. From the equations used in the method the expression given in Equation (3.2) is
derived. A more thorough analysis of the original equations is included in Appendix C.

From the expression derived from The Bournazel­Jeanson Method, it is observed that the
breakthrough time tBT is proportional to the porosity φ and inversely proportional to the
vertical permeability kv. For many of the remaining variables, the apparent proportionality
in Equation (3.2) should not be taken at face value as they are also used in the calculation
of the dimensionless cone height Z. However, tBT is positively correlated with Z, the oil
column height in the reservoir h and horizontal permeability kh. The breakthrough time
tBT is negatively correlated with the distance from the top of the reservoir to the bottom of
the perforations hp, the oil production rate Qo, the oil formation volume factor Bo and the
water­oil mobility M as the exponent α is positive. Lastly, it can be seen in Appendix C that
tBT is positively correlated with the water­oil density difference (ρw ´ ρo) and negatively
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
11
==PAGE:22==
Chapter 3. Theory
correlated with the viscosity of oil µo through Z. The variables Z, Bo and M are also
described in more detail in Appendix C.
The water coning phenomenon is affected by gravitational, viscous and capillary forces
though descriptions often consider capillary forces to have a negligible effect, which is
exemplified in Equation (3.2). Gravity influences many of the variables in this equation.
Intuitively, gravity helps to counterbalance the effect because of the difference in density,
which is greater in gas reservoirs compared with oil reservoirs. However, B0 depends on
the pressure which is increased by gravity which then lowers the breakthrough time.
As illustrated by the summary of the breakthrough time, descriptions of water coning re­
main fairly complex even in a simplified steady­state situation. Moreover, Equation (3.2)
did not account for the inhomogeneous nature of the reservoir.
In general, it is desirable to avoid water coning as it leads to increased water production
and this water has to be disposed of at the surface. Moreover, the extraction of water will
normally lead to a reduced gas production rate once the water cone has broken through to
the well’s perforations as illustrated in Figure 3.2a. If a well starts producing a significant
amount of water early in an extraction season, the well can be taken out of production for
some time to allow a potential water cone to level out. This is illustrated in Figure 3.2b.
Hence, it might be possible to use the well again later in the extraction season where it
should display a reduced water production rate compared with the time it was first taken
out of production. However, the production season may also be over by the time the cone
has levelled out.
Since water coning is a known issue in petroleum engineering a number of countermea­
sures exist. These include digging horizontal wells instead of vertical, establishing barriers
in the reservoir, limiting the section of the well that has perforation etc. (Okon et al. 2017,
[48]). However, many of these mechanical measures are not a possibility for an already
established facility, moreover, they are for the most part only suitable for purely extraction
plants.

the variance are both constant in time and the autocovariance depends only on the time

3.2.2
The ARIMA model
ARIMA and ARMA models are statistical linear models and are used to model univariate
time series data where the time series is a realisation of an underlying stochastic process.2
Starting with the ARMA model, this model is composed of two parts: an autoregressive
(AR) part:
Yt + φ1Yt´1 + ... + φpYt´p = εt,
where εt is a white noise process and a moving average (MA) part:
Yt = εt + θ1εt´1 + ... + θqεt´q.
This gives an ARMA(p, q) process:
Yt + φ1Yt´1 + ... + φpYt´p = εt + θ1εt´1 + ... + θqεt´q,
which can be written in terms of the backward shift operator as:
(1 + φ1L + ... + φpLp) Yt = (1 + θ1L + ... + θqLq) εt,
φ(L)Yt = θ(L)εt.
From this the transfer function is given as H(z) = θ(z´1)
φ(z´1). The process is stationary when
the roots of the polynomial φ(z´1) are all within the unit circle. If this is not the case, then
the process is non­stationary and differencing of the time series is used instead of the
time series itself. This process allows for the removal of a unit root from the polynomial
φ(z´1). The differencing transformation for order d is given as:
Wt = ∇dYt = (1 ´ L)d Yt,
1The following description of Wold’s decomposition is based on (Hamilton 1994, [22])
2The following description of the ARIMA model is based on Madsen 2007, [45]
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
13
==PAGE:24==
Chapter 3. Theory
which makes the ARMA(p, q) model into an ARIMA(p, d, q) model:
Wt + φ1Wt´1 + ... + φpWt´p = εt + θ1εt´1 + ... + θqεt´q,
(3.5)
φ(L)∇dYt = θ(L)εt.
(3.6)
An ARIMA model can also have seasonal variations, which are denoted by capital letters
giving an ARIMA(p, d, q) ˆ (P, D, Q) model.
If the ARIMA model describes the underlying process of a time series well then its resid­
uals (ˆεt) should resemble white noise, and a way to verify this is to inspect the autocor­
relation and partial autocorrelation of the residuals. The autocorrelation of white­noise
residuals for t1 ‰ t2 should not be significant.
The partial autocorrelation is a measure of a process’ correlation with itself to a lagged
time step, but conditioned on all the in­between time steps. The partial autocorrelation is
given by:
φ (t, t + k) = Corr[Yt, Yt+k|Yt+1, ..., Yt+k´1],
which for a wide sense stationary process is only dependant on the time difference k,
(Hyndman 2018, [31]). Here, white noise residuals should yield no significant partial au­
tocorrelation when k ‰ 0. Hence, the information from the autocorrelation and partial
autocorrelation of the residuals can give qualitative information on how to build the model.
3.2.3
Procedure for identifying an ARIMA model
The goal is to determine an ARIMA(p, d, q) ˆ (P, D, Q) model, potentially in conjunction
with a transformation, which describes the underlying process of the time series. The
model can be built in an iterative fashion according to the one described by George
E.
P.
Box 1976, [17], which is also known as the Box­Jenkins methodology.
Here,
simpler models are tried at first. These are then expanded to more complex ones if the
model is not satisfactory i.e. if the residuals are not similar enough to white noise. In this
thesis, the standard significance level p = 0.05 is used to determine significance of the
parameters in the models.
The first step is to decide if a transformation of the data is needed and what kind of trans­
formation is most applicable. A range­mean plot can be used to expose non­stationarity
of the variance, which guides the selection of a transformation. Secondly, the level of
differencing is determined by increasing the order of differencing until the ACF and PACF
both decrease sufficiently fast to zero. If differencing is used, there is no need to subtract
the mean of the data.
The next step is to identify the order of the autoregressive and moving average parts. This
is primarily done by investigating the structures of the ACF and PACF and the simplest
one dictates where to add a parameter. For instance, if ACF has the simplest structure
with significant correlations at two lags, then an MA term is added at the lag with the
most significant correlation. This development is based on the theoretical characteris­
tics of ACF and PACF of a stationary ARMA process given in Table 3.3, (Madsen 2007,
[45]). This procedure is repeated for the residuals of each estimated model, ideally, until
the residuals resemble white noise, i.e. they should be uncorrelated and have mean 0.
Hence, if either the ACF or the PACF of the residuals show significant correlations at other
lags than 0, then the order of the model should be increased. Using the significance level
p = 0.05 one should expect a few significant correlations to appear by chance but their
values should be small and they should be randomly distributed.
14
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:25==
3.2. ARIMA and multivariate extensions

When the ACF and PACF resemble white noise, then additional tests of the residuals can
be used. These include among others the Ljung­Box test, which tests for serial correlation,
the sign test that assesses if the frequency of sign changes is close to 1
2 and the QQ­plot
that compares the distribution to a normal distribution, (Madsen 2007, [45]).
If two or more models describe the training data reasonably well, i.e. the residuals are
similar to white noise, then they can be compared using the Akaike’s Information Criteria
(AIC) or Bayesian Information Criterion (BIC), (Madsen 2007, [45]).
AIC = ´2 log(max. likelihood) + 2 ni,
BIC = ´2 log(max. likelihood) + ni log (N) ,
Where N is the number of data points and ni is the number parameters, which is ni = p+q
in the ARMA(p, q) model.
As a last step, all the parameters in the model should be significantly different from zero.
This can be verified by checking if the value of the parameter lies inside an interval around
zero spanning twice the standard error. If the value is barely inside then a t­test should
be applied, (Madsen 2007, [45]). Obviously, excluding an insignificant parameter from a
model might cause the residual structure to be less than ideal. In this case, we would
choose the smaller model where all the parameters are significant.
The coefficients of the ARIMA models are determined using the `BK function in _ (R
Core Team 2013, [55]). This uses a BFGS­algorithm to compute the maximum of the
likelihood function as determined using the algorithm described by Gardner et al. 1980,
[16].
3.2.4
Predictions using ARIMA
The optimal predictor is the conditional mean: ˆYt+h|t = E [Yt+h|Yt, Yt´1, ...], (Madsen 2007,
[45]). When performing predictions using the ARIMA model, the t in Equation (3.5) is
replaced by t + h, where h is the prediction horizon. Future observations are replaced by
their forecasts meaning future residuals become zero.
Assuming that the residuals are normally distributed, a (1´α) confidence interval is given
by: ˆYt+h|t ˘ uα/2σh, with uα/2 being the α/2 quantile in the standard normal distribution
and σh being the standard deviation of the prediction error, (Madsen 2007, [45]). For a
95% confidence interval, α = 0.05 and u0.05/2 « 1.96.

==PAGE:26==

3.2.5
Multivariate extensions and K`BK
The data investigated in this thesis is multivariate and hence a multivariate extension of
the ARIMA model is of interest.3 There is a simple multivariate extension called ARMAX,
where an extra (or multiple) time series is added to the AR part of an ARMA model as
an exogenous variable. In the case of two time series, y1 and y2, from two stochastic
processes, the structure of the ARMAX model is:
Y1t + φ1Y1t´1 + ... + φpY1t´p + β1Y2t´1 + ... + βsY1t´s = εt + θ1εt´1 + ... + θqεt´q.
Differencing can be calculated for each variable and incorporated in the model if they are
not stationary. One shortcoming of this model is in forecasting more than 1­step ahead.
The exogenous variable is not forecasted, and therefore, the multi­step forecasts will be
based primarily on the endogenous variable. A more general multivariate extension of the
ARIMA model is the Vector ARIMA or VARIMA. This model includes both the interdepen­
dencies between the different variables as well as their potential individual differencing
terms, and all variables are forecasted. Now Yt represents a multivariate stochastic pro­
cess:

The differencing matrix contains only diagonal elements as the differencing transformation
of the variables should not be applied across different variables. As with the univariate
ARIMA model, the aim of the differencing is to make each process describing each time
series stationary. Therefore, univariate ARIMA models for each time series are deter­
mined, and thus, differencing terms will be known and ∇(L) can be constructed.
Any cross­correlation between the variables should be reflected in Φi and Θi as they
will then contain non­zero values in the off­diagonal elements. If the matrices Φi and
Θi only contain diagonal elements the whole system of equations will be equivalent to k
independent ARIMA models.
The tools in the K`BK package in R, (Spliid 2016, [69]), are used to estimate the model
parameters. In this package, a method based on repeated multivariate regression is ap­
plied. This method is computationally efficient, which is well suited for our multivariate time
series data set. The parameter estimation is not based on maximum likelihood, however,
empirical evidence shows that the estimated parameters are often close to those that
maximise the likelihood, (Spliid 1983, [68]).
3.3
Cross­correlation and pre­whitening
Cross­correlation is a measure of the correlation between two time series at different time
step displacements.4 The sample cross­covariance is given by:
Cy1,y2(k) = 1
N
N´k
ÿ
t=1
(y1t ´ ¯y1) (y2t+k ´ ¯y2) .
(3.9)
From this, the cross­correlation function is estimated as
ˆρy1,y2(k) =
Cy1,y2(k)
a
Cy1,y1(0)Cy2,y2(0).
However, using this to establish the cross­correlation between two time series can be
misleading if the time series are autocorrelated. Instead, pre­whitening can used to de­
termine the correlation as described by Madsen 2007, [45]. The first step is to identify a
suitable ARIMA model to describe y1t which results in white­noise residuals:
φ(L)y1t = θ(L)εt.
From this pre­whitening of y1t is performed to generate a white noise signal with no auto­
correlation:
εt = φ(L)
θ(L) y1t.
4This section is based on Madsen 2007, [45]
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
17
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 3. Theory
This pre­whitening is then also performed on the time series y2t:
βt = φ(L)
θ(L) y2t.
Now εt and βt can be used in the cross­correlation function to identify the cross­correlation.
The results may be compared with those of two uncorrelated time series, one of which is
a realisation of a white noise process, to test if the time series are correlated.
3.4
Spectral Analysis
A covariance stationary process may be represented both in the time domain and the
frequency domain. Any feature can be described by both representations though the
description in one of the domains may be simpler, (Hamilton 1994, [22]).
A signal with a regular periodic behaviour is likely to have a simple spectrum as this
describes the signal in terms of frequencies. However, if the signal is not periodic, it is
preferred to analyse the spectrum in shorter periods of the time as potential frequency
information during these periods might become less apparent than in the spectrum for the
full signal.
3.4.1
Short­time Fourier Transform (STFT)
The short­time Fourier Transform (STFT) may be used to determine the frequency content
of a signal over time. The discrete time STFT is given by:
X(n, k) =
8
ÿ
m=´8
x(m)w(m ´ n)e´i 2π
N km,
(3.10)
with w(m ´ n) being a window function. In practice, this is performed using fast Fourier
transform. The squared magnitude is the power spectral density which is visualised in a
spectrum, (Sherrick 2014, [64]).
Fourier series are used to approximate signals by an infinite sum of continuous sine
curves, however, this description is only practical if the number of curves used to approx­
imate a signal is finite. This applies well to continuous series, but using only finite sums
makes it difficult to approximate discontinuities. The result is the Gibb’s phenomenon
where excess amplitudes in the high­frequency terms result in a rippling effect near the
discontinuity. This increase in the frequency content will then be observed in the spectral
domain of the signal. For a single impulse signal, the STFT will return a constant value
at all frequencies times the window. The use of windows like Hanning or Hamming will
reduce the effect of increased frequencies at the boundaries of the windows at the cost
of reducing the amplitude of the signal, (Sherrick 2014, [64]).
A spectrogram of the water production from a well during an extraction season can be
seen in Appendix D. Here it is observed that the frequency domain of the water produc­
tion is experiencing the negative effects due to the discontinuous signal, as the water
production consists of many discrete measurements that are similar to impulses. There­
fore, we turned our attention away from the frequency domain.
3.5
Recurrent neural networks
Recurrent neural networks (RNNs) are a type of neural networks that are useful for mod­
elling temporal connections in data. For this reason, they are commonly used in connec­
tion with time series data, especially in forecasting. Parameter sharing across the time
steps helps RNNs model the sequential relationships in the data. In brief, RNNs work by
18
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:29==
3.5. Recurrent neural networks
having an internal hidden state ht´1, which is taken as input in every time step along with
the input features at the time step xt, (Graves 2012, [20]).
In regards to the overall architecture of RNNs, many variants exist often denoted by the
size of the input relative to the output. These include one­to­many, many­to­one, etc.
Moreover, the network may have a sequence­to­sequence (Seq2Seq) structure that com­
bines an encoder (many­to­one) and a decoder (one­to­many). A multilayered network
may be built by stacking RNNs on top of each other whereby a sequence of hidden states
from the previous layer is used as input for the next layer. This has the potential of creat­
ing very deep networks, however, only 2­5 layers are commonly used due to the difficulty
in training these deep RNNs, (Torres et al. 2020, [78]).
The transition function that describes the mapping from one time step to another also
has multiple variants. Among these are vanilla RNN and LSTM, which are outlined in
Section 3.5.1 and Section 3.5.2 respectively. Another common variant called GRU is
included in brief in Section 2.2.2 for completeness. Vanilla RNNs suffer from the vanishing
and exploding gradient problem. However, variants such as LSTM and GRU have been
applied successfully to a variety of problems from natural language processing to time
series forecasting.
It is worth noticing that in general, it may be very difficult to analyse the behaviour of a
neural network based on its weights due to the number of layers and the presence of
non­linearities. One possible way of analysing a network is to perform perturbations of
the input and evaluate how they affect the predictions, which in effect computes the local
derivatives. However, as pointed out by Graves 2012, [20] this method is limited by the
fact that the sensitivity of a network might be large towards inputs that never change.
Back­propagation through time
The weights in the neural network may be tuned by minimising the loss using numerical
optimisation This involves passing the input through the network to produce a prediction.
Form this, the loss and its gradients with respect to weights are computed. The compu­
tation of these gradients is done using backpropagation. Lastly, parameters are updated
using gradient descent.
In a recurrent neural network, the input is a sequence, so the total loss is the loss over all
time steps in the forward pass
L =
N
ÿ
t=1
Lt =
N
ÿ
t=1
L (yt, ˆyt) .
(3.11)
Computing the gradient of this loss with respect to all parameters in the network (θ) in­
volves first passing the whole input sequence through the network to produce the outputs
needed for the loss, and then a backward pass where the gradients from the last out­
put are propagated backwards to the first time step. Neither the forward pass nor the
backward pass can be executed in parallel as both procedures are inherently sequential
making the runtime O(N), (Goodfellow et al. 2016, [19]).
Dropout
Due to the computational cost of training a neural network, an ensemble of networks
is often considered infeasible. An alternative is provided by dropout, which conceptu­
ally corresponds to training an ensemble of subnetworks formed by removing non­output
units. The units are removed at random during training with a given probability known as
the dropout rate. This way, a fraction of the subnetworks is trained in each update step,
while the weights associated with the removed units are not adjusted. Therefore, applying
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
19
==PAGE:30==
Chapter 3. Theory
dropout to a network may result in an increased training time and a larger model to offset
the regularisation effect, (Goodfellow et al. 2016, [19]).
3.5.1
Vanilla RNN
In the vanilla RNN, the recurrence procedure that updates the internal state as well the
output ˆyt+1 are given by:
ht =f (xt, ht´1; θ) = tanh (U xt + W ht´1 + b) = tanh

where the activation function g may be the softmax function for classification or the identity
in a regression setting. It is seen that the same transition function f is applied in every
recurrence. Its parameters (θ), given by the weights (W, U) and the bias (b), have no
dependency on the time step t as they are shared across every time step. In principle,
this makes it possible to learn a model that generalises to sequences of any length. The
network may also produce an output in every recurrence by applying a set of weights and
an activation function to the hidden state, (Goodfellow et al. 2016, [19]).
In a vanilla RNN, the gradients with respect to the weights and biases depend on the
gradient of the loss function with respect to the hidden state ht. This gradient at a time

known as truncated backpropagation through time where a gradient step is performed
for a subsequence of inputs somewhat remedies this. Additionally, gradient clipping may
be used in practice to avoid exploding gradients. Another counter measure to the gradi­
ent problem is to use gates, which is the concept used for the LSTM and GRU blocks,
(Goodfellow et al. 2016, [19]).

3.5.2
Long Short­term Memory (LSTM)
The terminology used by various authors when describing LSTMs is not consistent. In
this thesis, we will refer to the basic building block shown in Figure 3.4 as an LSTM block.
Across the literature, many names have been applied to the LSTM block including ’LSTM
cell’ and ’LSTM unit’ among others, however, we will not use these terms here. An LSTM
block consists of multiple gates as well as a cell state a ct and a hidden state ht, which
are vectors of the same dimension. Some authors refer to gates as ’gate units’ and the
states as ’memory cells’ or ’memory units’, which are terms we will avoid using.

Figure 3.4: The sketch shows an LSTM block where the biases have not been included
for simplicity. The illustration was inspired by Theodoridis 2020, [76].
The LSTM block has three gates that control what is stored in the cell state. The activities
performed by the gates are commonly interpreted in the following way. The forget gate
(ft) controls what information is removed from the previous state while the input gate (it)
controls the new information to be added along with the last gate (gt). The output gate
(ot) determines the output from the block, (Goodfellow et al. 2016, [19]).
The goal of the cell state representation is to avoid vanishing and exploding gradients,
which happens in the vanilla RNN. The cell state acts in the same way skip connections
do for ResNet style CNNs in the sense that gradients may easily propagate between
LSTM blocks.

3.5.3
Gradient descent
Stating the loss as a function of the parameters L (θ) allows for minimisation of the loss
using gradient descent methods.6 In brief, these methods work by first initialising the
parameters θ randomly and then changing the parameter values slightly to decrease the
loss. This is achieved by performing updates of the form:
θt = θt´1 ´ α∇θL (θt´1) ,
until a local minimum is reached, which is where the gradient is zero. These gradients
are local and points in the direction of greatest ascent, so the step is taken in the negative
gradient direction. The step length α, also known as the learning rate, determines the
magnitude of the change in the weights. A suitable choice of step size is needed; if the
step length is too small, the convergence will be slow, while a too large step size results
in divergence. Also, the magnitude of oscillation in the loss which occurs near a local
minimum is linked to the step size.
In a neural network, the parameters θ are the weights and biases, and the loss function is
in general highly non­convex meaning many local minima exist. This complicates the op­
timisation procedure and necessitates multiple weight initialisations. The optimal weights
are then chosen as the ones that result in the lowest value of the loss function L in hopes
of finding a local minimum whose associated loss is not much different from that of the
global minimum. While the hope is to minimise the loss on the test set, it is achieved
indirectly by performing the optimisation on the training loss.
The gradients are computed using backpropagation of errors as described above. If this
loss is only computed for a single sequence, the optimisation will likely display much
variability while a computation of the loss based on the whole data set will make the
procedure very slow. A compromise between these two is mini­batch gradient descent,
where the gradients are averaged over a subset of all the sequences in the training set in
hopes of generating a reasonable estimate of the gradient for the whole data set. It also
allows the sequences within a mini­batch to be processed in parallel. During training,
the sequences are shuffled after every epoch to remove possible correlations between
successive sequences. This procedure may be problematic when performed on data from
all the gas wells, as the gradients from the different wells may point in different directions.
Adam
In this thesis, we use the Adam optimisation algorithm as proposed by Kingma et al. 2015,
[35] to tune the weights in our neural network. They show that Adam is a good choice for

learning rate for each parameter and a momentum update. The update at step t in the
Adam algorithm is given by Equation (3.19).
mt = β1mt´1 + (1 ´ β1) ∇θL (θt´1) ,

values by Kingma et al. 2015, [35]. A bias correction is applied in Equation (3.19c) as

3.6
Generalisation error and data partitioning
In order to develop the models used in this thesis, the data set is split into subsets. The
test set is only used for evaluation purposes, and the same test set is shared across all
models. The remaining data is used to develop the models.
The generalisation error (Errgen) of a prediction model ˆf(x) is the prediction error on un­
seen data. In the hold­out method used in this thesis, this quantity is approximated using
an empirical average of the loss on the test set.
Errgen = E(y,x)
-
L
.
y, ˆf(x)
/0
« Errtest =
1
Ntest
Ntest
ÿ
i=1
L
.
yi, ˆf(xi)
/
(3.20)
If the test set is large and drawn from the true distribution, the empirical average is close to
the true expectation. A related quantity is the expected prediction error where the training
set is also considered random, and this may be decomposed into a bias, a variance and
an irreducible term, (Hastie et al. 2001, [25]).
Ideally, the test error should be close to the training error. If the training error is much lower
than the test error, it indicates that the model has overfitted to the training data. In other
words, what the model has captured does not generalise to the underlying distribution.
A possible remedy is to apply a model with a larger bias, which can be achieved using
regularisation. Conversely, if the test error is close to the training error but both are far
from the true generalisation error, the bias of the model might be too large. Increasing the
variance may improve the performance if there is enough data to develop a model with
greater complexity. This is also known as the bias­variance trade­off. A large test set may
also make the model more prone to overfitting as less data is used for training, (Hastie
et al. 2001, [25]).
As described in Chapter 1, we want to construct a model, which is able to perform accu­
rate 7­day forecasts of the WPR for the wells at Stenlille. Therefore, the data from each
well is split into a training and a test set, where the test set includes the most recent data.
In Section 4.6.1, it is described how the level of extraction varies across the years, which
24
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:35==
3.7. Evaluation metric
makes it sensible to partition the data set into homogeneous sub­populations. For non­
time series data, a reasonable strategy is then to apply stratified sampling when splitting
the data. However, the models used in this thesis are time series models. These are
based on sequentially ordered data with temporal dependencies that may result in infor­
mation leaking between the splits, which will produce an underestimation of the error.
Therefore, consecutive splits are used where we try to split the data such that the distinct
sub­populations are represented within each split.
The methods used for developing the ARIMA and the K`BK models only require a single
training split for estimating the parameters. The methods used in connection with a recur­
rent neural networks are summarised in Section 5.4. Here, a validation set is required in
addition to the training set, and the validation set is used to tune the hyperparameters for
this type of model. Hence, the training set used for the ARIMA and the K`BK models is
split further into a validation set and a new, smaller training set to be used for the LSTM
class of models.
3.7
Evaluation metric
3.7.1
RMSE
The accuracy of the forecasts are mainly measured using the root mean square error
(RMSE), (Hyndman 2018, [31]). The RMSE for a well or zone (Б) is given by:

Where NБ is the number of observations for the well or zone (Б) in the given split. This

This metric is only concerned with point forecasts and does not carry with it any information
about the prediction intervals.
Normalised RMSE
In addition to the ”raw” root mean square error described in Section 3.7.1, the normalised
RMSE is also used to evaluate the forecasts. The normalisation is performed with respect
to the RMSE of a mean model where the mean has been computed on the training set
for each well or zone (Б). When computing the training error, this normalisation is equal
to the standard deviation of the training set. Therefore, normalised RMSE is given by:

A model that only estimates the mean will have a normalised RMSE equal to 1 on both

parison of models for different wells and zones become easier. However, these sorts of
comparisons have their limitations as the various time series may exhibit different features
that could make it more or less difficult to attain a lower error than the mean model.

==PAGE:36==
Chapter 3. Theory
3.8
Naïve model: Random walk
To evaluate the quality of the initial models developed in this thesis, their forecasts are
compared with the forecasts of a simple random walk model. The random walk may be
written as:
yt+1, = yt + εt+1,
where εt is white noise errors (Madsen 2007, [45]). In this model, every forecast is re­
placed by the last observed value. The h­step forecast for the well or zone Б is given
by:
ˆy(t+h, Б) = y(t, Б).
The results from this model are shown in Appendix F, and the comparison will be most
interesting for the ARIMA models, as the random walk model can be expressed as a
simple ARIMA model with one order of differencing.
3.9
Statistical comparison of models
The error on the test set is an estimate of the generalisation error of the model. A statistical
test such as a t­test may be used to determine if a difference in the residuals between two
models is significant. Under the assumption that the difference in squared residuals is
normally distributed, they follow a Student’s t­distribution with Ntest´1 degrees of freedom.
From this a 95 % confidence interval and a p­value is calculated where the p­value is
based on the null hypothesis that the models have equal performance, i.e. there is no
difference in the residuals of the compared models, (Hastie et al. 2001, [25]).
3.9.1
False Discovery Rate
Due to the number of wells treated in this thesis, each of which is likely to require a
specifically tuned model, many pairwise tests are performed.7 Using the p­value for each
test to determine significance is likely to produce a large number of false positives, which
may be quantified using the False Discovery Rate (FDR):

4
Data
A description of the data set is provided in this chapter. This includes an overview of
the variables and their physical origin as well as general considerations regarding the
operation of the facility, which influences the variables. Data cleaning is also described in
this section as well as the partition of the data set into training, validation and test sets.
4.1
Total gas and working gas
The amount of gas that can be extracted from the reservoirs is less than the total volume
of gas in the storage facility, as part of the gas becomes permanently embedded in the
porous sandstone making it physically unrecoverable. The working gas is the quantity
of gas, which can be readily extracted at any given moment. An amount of gas called
cushion gas is needed for the extraction of gas as the cushion and embedded gas help
maintain adequate pressures for recovering the working gas. The total amount of gas and
the expected working gas for the storage year 1 May 2019 to 30 April 2020 can be seen
in Table 4.1. It is seen that there is around 2.6 times more working gas in Zone 5 when
the plant is at full capacity.
Table 4.1: Total gas and expected working gas in storage at 100% capacity for the storage
year 1 May 2019 to 30 April 2020 (Stade et al. 2020, [70]).
Total gas
Expected working gas

4.2
Overview of the data set and the variables
The data set used in this thesis consists of measurements from 1 October 2009 to 26
April 2021 both days included. This corresponds to 11 complete seasons where both
injection and extraction seasons are present. There is 1 additional extraction season at
the beginning of the data set where the previous injection season is not present.
Each of the 14 operational wells measures the variables given in Table 4.2. Here, the
tubing refers to the tube through which the production fluids travel, and the annulus is the
void between the tubing and its casing (A) or between casings (B). The current distribution
of wells between the two reservoirs, which is listed Table 1.2 in Section 1.1, has remained
unchanged since the perforations of ST­17 were moved from Zone 1­3 to Zone 5 on 26
March 2010. Between 1 October 2009 and 26 March 2010 perforations were also moved
to Zone 5 for ST­7 and ST­13. ST­20 also became functional within this period of time.
Therefore, the time series from ST­7, ST­13, ST­17 and ST­20 are shorter than the rest,
(Stade et al. 2020, [70]).
The data is stored in 5­minute samples in GSD’s database. These 5­minute samples are
calculated as the average value of 10­second samples. The database also contains a
number of derived variables that are computed based on the measured variables. From
the gas rates and water production, four cumulative variables, one binary variable and
one ratio variable are computed. The derived variables are listed in Table 4.3.

4.3
Sketch of a well with sensor locations
A simplified sketch of a well is shown in Figure 4.4, which illustrates where the sensors
measuring the various variables are located.1 In the lower left corner of Figure 4.4, the
well’s perforations to the gas reservoir are shown. The well head is located at the top of
this pipe just above ground level with a master valve, which is open when gas is either
extracted or injected and closed otherwise. The pressure sensor is situated between the
master valve and the reservoir, and the temperature sensor is located between the master
valve and the free water separator (FWS).
During extraction, the gas enters the FWS where potential free water, extracted as a
byproduct from the reservoir, is separated from the gas. The water is drained through an
automatic valve, which is controlled by the sensors LSH (level switch high) and LSL (level
switch low). The automatic valve opens when the water level reaches the LSH sensor
and closes again when the water level drops below the LSL sensor. WPR is calculated
using the opening times of the automatic valve and this process is covered in detail in
Section 4.5.3. After the automatic valve, there is a manual valve that will also be described
in Section 4.5.3. The flow rates of the extracted and injected gas are measured outside
the FWS, in the pipe connecting the well to the main gas plant. When gas is injected, it
does not pass through the FWS, however, the bypass is omitted in the sketch for simplicity.
It should be noted that the extracted gas still contains water vapour after leaving the FWS,
and this is removed at the main plant using glycol. However, during periods of substantial
water production, the majority of the extracted water is collected in the FWS. All the wells
1The information in this section is the result of a tour of the facilities at GSD.

are connected to the main gas plant, and the main gas plant is connected to the Danish
gas transmission network.
Figure 4.4: Sketch of a well and its corresponding free water separator with annotations
denoting where the measurements of the variables are performed.
4.4
Similarities between wells
As previously mentioned, GSD operates 14 wells across two reservoirs.2 These two reser­
voirs are separated by a clay layer, which prevents gas flow between them. Therefore,
wells located in different reservoirs are not expected to share many physical characteris­
tics.
For wells with perforations to the same reservoir, the most telling feature of similarity is the
physical location of the perforations within the reservoir. For all wells, these are located
close to the cap rock so the greatest variation is seen for the horizontal displacement of the
perforations. Along these lines, the behaviour of the individual well is affected by whether
it is located in the centre of the reservoir or at the edge. The general flow of the gas has
been observed to be from north­east to south­west, which is also worth taking into account
when judging the proximity of the wells. The general direction of the flow also accounts
for GSD’s previous strategy, which was to primarily inject gas in north­eastern wells and
extract it from the south­western wells. For the past few years, the overall strategy has
been a more balanced operation where approximately the same amount of gas is injected
and extracted from the same well.
Faults within the reservoir may also be influential in determining similarities between wells
as they represent discontinuities in the physical characteristics. Two faults divide the
reservoir into three compartments. Gas flow between these compartments are known to
occur especially across one of the faults, however, the exact extent of the exchange is not
known. ST­9 has been dug into a fault so it is rarely used and produces much water when
2The following description of the wells is based on Stade et al. 2020, [70].

==PAGE:40==
Chapter 4. Data
used. The compartments along with the location of the wells are shown in Appendix A.3.
Taking all of this into account, the wells may roughly be grouped as shown in Table 4.5
though it is also reasonable to combine the four wells ST­7, ST­8, ST­13 and ST­14 in one
group as they are all fairly similar. It should also be noted that this grouping reflects both
physical characteristics and operational patterns.

4.5
Data variables used for the modelling task
The following section is an outline of the variables included in the modelling task. The
included variables are GIR, GPR, WPR, THP, THT and netINJ. The remaining variables
have been omitted, and these are discussed in Appendix E.2 along with the reasons for
not including them. The included variables have been acquired from GSD’s database in
5­minute samples. Data in this resolution is used to discuss and visualise the different
variables in this section. It will become apparent that the variables WPR, THP and THT
contain several outliers due to erroneous measurements. Therefore, cleaning of these
variables was necessary before including them in the models.
It is worth pointing out that any data cleaning along with imputation of missing variables
should in principle be guided by the training set only. Using knowledge from the test
set may transfer information from the test set to the training set. This may result in an
underestimation of the generalisation error as the test set no longer represents unknown
data. In a similar vein, any statistics that might guide the choice of models should only be
performed on the training set as basing the model development on the whole data set will
introduce a bias. However, the bias introduced this way is assumed to be negligible.
30
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:41==
4.5. Data variables used for the modelling task
4.5.1
Gas injection rate (GIR) and gas production rate (GPR)
GIR and GPR are measured using ultrasonic flow meters, which compute the velocity
from the Doppler effect. These measurements are important for GSD as they are used
to monitor the amount of gas in the reservoirs. The production and injection rates are
set directly by GSD and are controlled using valves and compressors, which provide a
pressure difference relative to the well heads. Hence, the actual gas rates vary slightly
relative to the desired rates set by GSD. The distribution of non­zero GIR and GPR for
all wells are shown in Figure 4.6. In the figure, the GPR seems to follow a unimodal
distribution with a peak around 15 000 Nm3/h, while GIR seems to follow a multimodal
distribution where the peaks are not easily identified. Using knowledge of the desired gas
injection rates from Krogh 2020, [37], it is understood that the major peak in the distribution
is the result of the primary injection rate of 10 000 Nm3/h. Also, the rates 15 000 Nm3/h
and 20 000 Nm3/h are used for injection, and these two peaks are also observed in the
distribution. From the observed distributions of GIR and GPR, no obvious outliers are
visible in the histograms.
Figure 4.6: The histogram shows the distribution of non­zero GIR and non­zero GPR for
all the wells. The gas rates has been rounded to the nearest bin.
GIR and GPR are plotted in Figure 4.7 along with netINJ for the well ST­18. This illustrates
the seasonal operational pattern of these variables for a well, which is frequently used in
production. From the figure, it is seen that the gas injection is primarily performed in the
summer season as described in Section 1.1. From the description of water coning in
Section 3.1.1, the total gas injection is suspected to be correlated with the total amount
of gas that can be extracted from the well in the subsequent season, before it starts to
produce water. However, the importance of the trajectory from the injection in predicting
the water production depends largely on the flow in the reservoir, which is only known in
general terms. Nonetheless, gas recently injected in a given well, is expected to be of
greater importance to the production of that particular well than gas injected earlier, as
the latter has had more time to disperse.
From Figure 4.7, it is seen that the gas production is primarily performed in the winter
season as described in Section 1.1. As water production can only occur in the presence
of gas production, this variable is assumed to be very important for forecasting the water
production. The gas production is preferably kept at a steady rate through periods of gas
demand. For most of the wells, the production flow rates may be adjusted, however, ST­2,
ST­7, ST­8, ST­9 and ST­14 do not a have a flow control valve (FCV) (Krogh 2020, [37]).
