==PAGE:42==
Chapter 4. Data
Figure 4.7: GIR, GPR and netINJ of ST­18 for the whole data set.
4.5.2
Net injection (netINJ)
As mentioned, in Section 4.2, netINJ equals cumGIR ´ cumGPR, and no direct cleaning
is needed for netINJ as it is derived from GIR and GPR. When data is extracted from the
database, the initial value of netINJ is set to zero, and the net injection is measured from
this point forward.
From Figure 4.7, netINJ can be seen to increase or decrease according to the injection
or extraction of gas. For this particular well, netINJ is seen to exhibit a general upward
trend as ST­18 has been used more for gas injection than gas extraction owing to its
north­eastern location within the reservoir. Other wells may extract more gas than they
inject, so their netINJ decrease over time. Similarly, wells with a more balanced operation
will neither display an upward nor a downward trend in netINJ. This last case also holds
true for the gas plant as a whole where the netINJ have the same seasonal patterns as
the individual wells. This can be seen in Figure 4.21 in Section 4.6. As described in
Section 3.1.1, water production is more prone to happen when the quantity of working
gas is low, which coincides with a low net injection. Therefore, netINJ is probably useful
in forecasting the water production.
4.5.3
Water production rate (WPR)
The water production rate is computed from the opening time of the automatic valve in the
FWS shown in Figure 4.4. The flow rate is assumed constant and it is estimated by GSD
to be 9.72 m3/h, though the details of how this value was determined are not well­known.
Therefore, the WPR is a scaling of the time it takes for the water level to drop from LSH
to LSL when the valve is open, which may vary depending on how much water pours into
the FWS, while the valve is open. Measuring the opening time of the valve instead of the
actual water production also introduces a lag, which may be large when the true water
production rate is low, however, in periods with large WPR, this lag is negligible and these
periods are also the most interesting in regards to water breakthrough in a well.
The data is stored as five­minute samples, so the largest volume of water discharged
during one sample is 9.72 m3/h¨1/12 h= 0.81 m3. This is close to the volume of the FWS,
which is listed as 0.8 m3 in Appendix A.1. It is possible that this has motivated the choice
of the maximum flow rate though the sensors which regulate the opening of the automatic
valve are separated by a volume less than 0.8 m3.
32
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:43==
4.5. Data variables used for the modelling task
30 distinct values of WPR, which are all multiples of 0.324 m3/h, are present in the data
across all wells except for ST­19.
The original live data was down­sampled from 10
seconds between samples to 5 minutes between samples before it was stored in the
database. This results in 31 unique combinations for the binary variable which encodes
whether the valve was open or closed, including the state where the valve remained closed
for the entire five­minute interval.
Figure 4.8: The histogram shows the distribution of the raw non­zero WPR for all wells
except ST­19 as its WPR has a higher resolution than the rest of the wells.
For ST­19, the measured WPR has a higher resolution and contains 1800 distinct values,
which corresponds to a variable with 60 distinct values sampled every 10 seconds in a
five­minute interval. When inquiring about the measurement of the water production rate,
it was explained that the flow value was computed using a running average of 60 seconds
with a step step size of 1 second, which accounts for the nature of the water production
rate for the well ST­19. However, judging from the data, this procedure only seems to be
implemented for this particular well.
The distribution of non­zero WPR measurements for all wells except ST­19 are shown
in Figure 4.8. Apart from the largest measurement of 9.72 m3/h, which occurs when
the automatic valve is open during the entire 5­minute interval, the distribution seems
to be unimodal with a peak at 0.6 m3/h. Also, the water production rates are usually
below 2 m3/h. 8032 maximum measurements are observed, and this amount seems
erroneous when compared with the general distribution in Figure 4.8. GSD informed us
of three known situations where the automatic valve is open, but water is not actually being
emptied from the FWS, which could account for the faulty maximum measurements. The
three scenarios are:
1. After an extraction season, the FWS is emptied of all remaining water. However,
the automatic valve is not hermetic so without a sheet of water covering the bottom
of the container, the gas leaks. Therefore, the manual valve shown in the bottom
right of Figure 4.4 is closed to avoid gas leaks. Typically, at the beginning of the next
extraction season, when the water level rises above the LSH sensor, the automatic
valve opens and water discharging is registered, but the closed manual valve pre­
vents the water from leaving the FWS. This causes maximum WPR measurements
to be recorded until the manual valve is opened.
2. The gas hydrates mentioned in Section 3.1 may clog the pipes which stops the
water flow and keep the automatic valve open, resulting in erroneous maximum
WPR measurements.
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
33
==PAGE:44==
Chapter 4. Data
3. Each well has its own FWS. These are connected to a manifold that leads to a
common water tank for the well site. Therefore, if one well produces a significant
amount of water, this may cause a high pressure in the manifold. If the manifold
pressure exceeds the pressure from another well’s FWS, this may prevent the sec­
ond well from discharging its water, and the automatic valve will stay open resulting
in erroneous maximum WPR measurements from this well.
Each well has a ’Flow Max Alarm’ to shut down water production automatically if a WPR
threshold is exceeded. The WPR thresholds for each well are listed in Appendix A.2. If
the one­minute running mean value of the WPR exceeds this threshold, then the GPR is
automatically lowered to the minimum value. If the one­minute running mean continuous
to exceed the threshold for a period of 15­20 minutes depending on the well, the gas
production of that well will shut down automatically. Hence, if maximum WPR is measured
for periods longer than 20 minutes, the ’Flow Max Alarm’ has shut down gas production,
and non­zero WPR measurements following this shutdown are known to be erroneous and
likely caused by one of the three scenarios described above. It is also possible that faulty
measurements result in values other than the maximum but judging from the distribution
in Figure 4.8, this seems to be a minor issue, and it would likely only manifest itself at the
end­points of an interval containing maximum measurements.
WPR data cleaning
As the purpose of this thesis is to forecast the WPR, it is crucial that this variable is properly
cleaned. As suggested above, when the maximum water production rate of 9.72 m3/h
is observed, the measurement might be erroneous. Following an inspection of the data,
five different patterns for the occurrence of maximum WPR measurements have been
identified. These patterns have been discussed with GSD to determine whether they
were valid or erroneous measurements.
In this section, these five patterns are examined and the faulty observations are adjusted.
In the cases, where the actual water production is believed to be zero, the WPR is re­
moved. For the ones, where a water production of unknown intensity is thought to have
occurred, the measurement is replaced by the median of 0.972 m3/h, which is also as­
sumed to correspond roughly to one drainage of the FWS. If consecutive faulty maximum
measurements occurred, only the first was set to the median while the rest were removed
as the FWS has likely only been drained once leaving the valve open. The choice of the
mean value for imputation is also considered reasonable by GSD.
Figure 4.9: GPR and WPR for ST­1. This is an example of Pattern one.
34
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:45==
4.5. Data variables used for the modelling task
Pattern one: The first type of maximum WPR measurement is observed after periods
with both gas and water production. Shortly after the maximum WPR is observed, the
gas production shuts down according to the flow alarm system, and the water produc­
tion ceases. An example of this pattern is shown in Figure 4.9 for ST­1. This kind of
maximum WPR measurement is believed to resemble the true water production during
breakthrough, which is confirmed by GSD. Hence, this type of maximum measurement is
not altered.
Pattern two: The second type of maximum WPR measurement is observed during spring
or summer in periods with no gas production. This is due to the FWS being emptied after
an extraction season. As there is no gas production, water production cannot occur and
the true value of WPR should be zero. This type of maximum WPR measurement is shown
in Figure 4.10 for ST­1, where there was no gas production in the 6 previous weeks. There
is also a minor WPR measurement a few days after the maximum observation, which is
also regarded as an erroneous measurement. The blue line shows the cleaned WPR
data, where the WPR measurements in periods with no gas extraction are set to zero.
It is worth pointing out that any water present in the FWS by the end of an extraction
season have been produced earlier. For this reason, prior WPR should in principle be
increased by this amount, which is not known as the valve is normally kept open longer
than needed. The quantity is likely very small as it has not activated the LSH. Also, it is not
known when the water was actually produced. For these reasons, the water production
rates of prior measurements are not increased.
Figure 4.10: GPR and WPR for ST­1. This is an example of Pattern two.
Pattern three: The third type of maximum WPR measurement is related to the first ob­
served water production in the extraction season. This maximum measurement is due to
the issue of the closed manual valve, which causes the automatic valve to remain open
described above in Section 4.5.3. This maximum measurement is an error, but should
not be set to zero as enough water has been produced to trigger the LSH sensor. The
maximum measurements of WPR continuous until the manual valve is opened by a tech­
nician. Therefore, all WPR observations are set to zero except the first value, which is set
to the median value of WPR (0.972 m3/h). In Figure 4.11, an example of this instance is
shown for ST­1, where the maximum WPR is seen to last for several 5­minute samples.
Pattern four: The fourth type of maximum WPR measurements is observed as multiple
maximum measurements occurring in a sequence within a short period of time. GSD
speculate that these measurements might be caused by tests, maintenance or gas hy­
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
35
==PAGE:46==
Chapter 4. Data
Figure 4.11: GPR and WPR for ST­1 in 2016. This is an example of Pattern three.
drates causing a continuous opening of the automatic valve as described above in Sec­
tion 4.5.3. Nevertheless, GSD is certain that the pattern does not reflect the operational
pattern of the facility. An example of this behaviour is seen in Figure 4.12 where 5 con­
secutive maximum measurements of WPR are observed over the course of two days for
ST­13. Gas production is seen to both precede and follow these measurements. Hence,
it is unlikely that the well has reached water breakthrough and the WPR is cleaned by
setting the first measurement in each of the maximum peaks to the median value of WPR
and the rest to zero.
Figure 4.12: GPR and WPR for ST­13. This is an example of Pattern four.
Pattern five: The fifth type of maximum WPR measurement is observed as extended
periods where the water production assumes its maximum value. This is exemplified in
Figure 4.13, where maximum WPR is observed over a period of multiple hours for ST­13.
These are clearly faulty observations as the flow alarm system shuts down the gas produc­
tion and thus water production cannot occur hereafter. In the figure, this cutoff happens
on the 2nd of February. The continued water production is probably caused by either the
closed manual valve if it happens at the beginning of the season or alternatively by gas
hydrates if it occurs later in the season. WPR is cleaned by setting the first measurement
to the median value of WPR and zeroing the rest.

The insights into the maximum WPR measurements from the preceding discussion are
used to develop a cleaning algorithm, which adjusts the erroneous WPR measurements
to either zero or to the the median value of WPR depending on the pattern. In periods of
faulty maximum WPR measurements, the observations surrounding the interval are also
cleaned by the algorithm. This is because of the sampling procedure used to obtain the
data as it is unlikely that a period of faulty measurements is a multiple of 5 minutes. This
algorithm is included in Appendix E.3.1, where the parts relating to the five patterns have
been pointed out.

wells except ST­19 as its WPR has a much higher resolution than the rest of the wells.

that the many erroneous maximum WPR measurements have been cleaned without alter­
ing the distribution of the remaining WPRs. A specific result of the WPR cleaning algorithm
applied to the well ST­20 is included in Appendix E.3.1
4.5.4
Tubing head pressure (THP)
As illustrated in the sketch in Figure 4.4, the pressure sensor is positioned between the
reservoir and master valve. Therefore, when the well is not in use, the sensor measures
the reservoir pressure minus the pressure from the gas column stretching from the perfo­
rations at the reservoir to the well head. Hence, the overall trend of the THP varies with
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
37
==PAGE:48==
Chapter 4. Data
the amount of gas in the reservoir, which gives it a seasonal trend similar to that of net­
INJ. This can be seen in Figure 4.15 where the cleaned THP is plotted along with the net
injection for ST­12. The cleaned THP is shown for ease of comparison, and the cleaning
procedure for the THP will be discussed later in this section.
Figure 4.15: Cleaned THP for ST­12 plotted alongside netINJ.
In Figure 4.15, it is seen that the purple THP curve is shifted to the left relative to the
netINJ curve meaning that the peaks and troughs often occur earlier for the pressure.
The reason being that after injection, part of the injected gas remains immediately around
the perforations for some time before it disperses as described in Section 3.1. There­
fore, the measured pressure only accounts for the reservoir pressure in a neighbourhood
surrounding the well’s perforations. In the same way, extraction of the gas will result in a
lower pressure around the well perforations, and the pressure will then increase over time
as the gas disperses in the reservoir. This mechanism also explains why the pressure is
sometimes seen to change without any change in the netINJ. This is visible in both 2013
and 2014, where the extraction season is followed by a period of inactivity. Also in the
middle of 2010, the pressure is seen to decrease in spite of an increasing netINJ as the
gas injection rate has been lowered after a period with a large injection. The injection
rates can be inferred from the reduced slope of the netINJ.
These oddities in Figure 4.15 might be explained by the finite permeability in the reservoir
perhaps in conjunction with the dynamical pressure drop that takes place when the rates
are lowered. However, it is worth remembering that the curves are only related to one
of the four wells in Zone 1­3 and the other wells are probably needed for explaining the
trajectory in full.
In Figure 4.15, smaller fluctuations in THP during periods of either gas injection or gas
production are also observed in addition to the overall cyclical behaviour. To investigate
this further, THP in the spring of 2018 is plotting alongside GIR, GPR and WPR for ST­
20 in Figure 4.16. In this figure, fluctuations in the THP are observed in April when gas
is injected. This occurs because the master valve is opened and a potential pressure
difference between the tubing head and the pressure set by the compressor will take a few
moments to stabilise. In the first half of the figure, variations in the gas production rate are
seen to be related to changes in THP. Several mechanisms may be involved here. Among
others, the movement of the gas in the pipe and reservoir involves some friction, which
causes small deviations in the gas column pressure. THP is also influenced by WPR as
the pressure is observed to experience an extra pressure drop during the two continuous
water production periods in March. This might be explained by the increased density in
38
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:49==
4.5. Data variables used for the modelling task
Figure 4.16: Cleaned THP for ST­20 plotted alongside GIR, GPR and WPR.
the pipe while the water is extracted from the reservoir, which increases the pressure drop
in the gas column. Hence, as THP and WPR share some interdependencies and THP
also gives insight into the amount of gas in the reservoir, THP might contain valuable
information for the task of forecasting the WPR.
The distribution of the raw THP measurement for all the wells is shown as green bars in
Figure 4.17. Most of the values lie within the interval [120 bara, 150 bara] with a peak
around 140 bara and another smaller one close to 135 bara. The peak at 1 bara is erro­
neous measurements caused by service work where the pressure sensor is exposed to
the ambient pressure, which is 1 bara at ground level pressure. During longer services,
the pressure sensor is often turned off giving rise to NaN values in the data. A total of
289 415 NaN values are logged in the data. Hence, data cleaning needs to be performed
on this variable.
Figure 4.17: The histogram shows the distribution of the raw and cleaned THP for all the
wells. The measurements are rounded to the nearest bin. All measurements from 10
bara to 90 bara are shown at ”[10, 90]”, and all measurements above 155 bara are shown
at ”155+”.
THP data cleaning
The THP measurements are also known to be prone to errors by GSD and genuine obser­
vations lie within a narrow pressure interval. Observations that fulfil one of the following
three conditions are known to be errors.
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
39
==PAGE:50==
Chapter 4. Data
1. THP values below a threshold of 100 bara.
2. THP values below 120 bara without the presence of gas production or gas injection.
This threshold is based on the seasonal trend that does not drop below 120 bara
except in periods with gas flow, which may cause fluctuations in the pressure.
3. THP values exceeding 150 bara. As stated by Krogh 2020, [37], the well head
pressure should never exceed this limit.
The two lower limits in condition 1 and 2 are a conservative estimate to avoid removing
correct low measurements. Valid measurements above 150 bara can occur if GSD ex­
ceeds their own restriction, but this rarely happens so the value in condition 3 is generally
a sensible upper limit.
THP values that fulfil at least one of the three conditions are replaced by NaN values. As
for WPR, the THP values right before and right after faulty THP measurements are also set
to NaN values, because these values are likely also corrupted. Subsequently, all the NaN
values are imputed with a linear interpolation using the endpoints surrounding the missing
observations, which is also considered reasonable by GSD. This procedure conserves
the temporal dependence that would be lost if a mean imputation was used, however, it
may introduce a trend that was not present in the data previously. The distribution of the
cleaned THP for all the wells is shown as the purple bars in Figure 4.17. The imputed
values are for the most part observed to coincide well with the overall distribution.
Erroneous THP observations of all three types are shown in Figure 4.18 for ST­1. The
THP observation in mid September 2011 is above 100 bara, but satisfies condition 2
and is removed as a result hereof. In March 2012, the THP drops to 1 bara followed
by an extended period of both 1 bara measurements and NaNs around April. The 1
bara measurements are all removed due to condition 1. The spike around August 2012
is removed because of condition 3. The purple curve, shows the cleaned THP where
the linear interpolation has been performed. As seen from the cleaning performed on
the samples from March to August, the periods imputed with linear interpolation may be
quite long. However, the erroneous measurements are often a result of service, which
happens while the well is inactive. Therefore, the pressure in the well is expected to
remain relatively stable meaning any trend induced by the imputation is likely small.
Figure 4.18: Raw and cleaned THP for ST­1 shown alongside GPR and GIR.
40
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:51==
4.5. Data variables used for the modelling task
The algorithm developed for cleaning THP along with a specific result of the cleaning
algorithm applied to the well ST­7 can be seen in Appendix E.3.2.
4.5.5
Tubing head temperature (THT)
As illustrated in the sketch of a well in Figure 4.4, the temperature sensor is positioned
between the master valve and the FWS. In the absence of gas flow, the sensor measures
the outdoor temperature. Furthermore, the sensor is sensitive to thermal radiation from
sunbeams during the daytime. Hence, when the well is inactive, the overall trend of THT
follows seasonal and daily variations in the temperature with the additional influence of the
sunlight. During periods of gas production or injection, the temperature sensor roughly
measures the temperature of the gas in the pipe. As the actual gas flow is already en­
coded in GIR and GPR, THT is thought to be the least important of the included variables.
Moreover, no direct relationship between the temperature and the water production is
known to exist.
The distribution of the raw THT measurements for all the wells is shown as the green bars
in Figure 4.19, where it can be seen that THT mostly lies within the interval [­5 °C, 40 °C].
The distribution is seen to be multimodal, and it appears to consist of four separate dis­
tributions. The first distribution with its peak at 7 °C describes the temperature during the
cold part of the year, while the second distribution with its peak at 14.5 °C describes the
temperature during the warm part of the year. Hence, these two peaks at lower tempera­
tures are explained by the periods with no gas flow. The gas in the pipe has a temperature
of around 29 °C during production and 33 °C during injection. Therefore, the periods of
gas production and injection explain the two peaks observed at larger temperatures.
In Figure 4.19, two types of erroneous measurements can be observed. The first type
is low measurements that may reach ­50 °C, which seems a bit too cold for a Danish
winter. Secondly, The amount of zero measurements seems too large compared with
the general shape of the distribution. As with the THP, These are due to maintenance,
where the sensor was turned off resulting in zero measurements being logged in the data.
Therefore, this variable is also cleaned prior to use.
Figure 4.19: The histogram shows the distribution of the raw and cleaned THT for all the
wells. The measurements are rounded to the nearest bin. All measurements below ­20
°C are shown at ”­20˚”.
THT data cleaning
The following two conditions for erroneous THT measurements were agreed on with GSD.
1. THT values below ­15 °C.
2. THT values that are exactly equal to 0 °C.
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
41
==PAGE:52==
Chapter 4. Data
All THT measurements fulfilling at least one of the conditions were removed and imputed
with a linear interpolation as for the THP. Only a few sequential erroneous measurements
were observed in the five­minute data. As for WPR and THP, the THT values in the
immediate vicinity of faulty THT measurements were also cleaned. The cleaning algorithm
for THT is included in Appendix E.3.2.
The distribution of the cleaned THT for an aggregate of all the wells is shown as grey
bars in Figure 4.19. Here, the very low temperature measurements have been removed
and the number of THT measurements at zero is reduced, which seems to be in congru­
ence with the overall distribution of THT. Similarly, a small peak around 44 °C has been
removed, which was probably due to faulty measurement preceding or following obser­
vations fulfilling condition 1 or 2. Lastly, many of the imputed values are 14.5 °C and this
may be explained by the prevalence of erroneous values in summer when the majority of
maintenance takes place.
An example of the cleaning algorithm is shown in Figure 4.20, where it is applied to the
THT of ST­16. The raw and cleaned THT are plotted alongside GPR and GIR. The pro­
cedure is seen to remove many of the sudden drops to zero including a few very low
measurements around the beginning of January 2016.
Figure 4.20: Raw and cleaned THT for ST­16 plotted alongside GPR and GIR.
4.6
Identifying extraction seasons
The yearly operational cycle of the storage facility results in an extraction season in winter.
These extraction seasons are identified based on the total net injection of the whole facility
as the period from the largest maximum in netINJ during summer to the lowest minimum
in the following winter. This definition of the extraction seasons is also used by GSD.
In Figure 4.21, the net injection is shown for the whole gas plant across all seasons in the
data set. The maximum in each season is highlighted in green and this denotes the start
of an extraction season. Similarly, the minimum net injection for the facility is shown in
red, which indicates the end of an extraction season. Therefore, there are a total of 12
extraction seasons in the data set. As we are particularly interested in predicting the water
production during the extraction seasons, these seasons are also used for evaluation
purposes in this thesis. A short period immediately following each season is known as
the shoulder season where the facility alternates between gas injection and extraction.
42
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:53==
4.6. Identifying extraction seasons
Figure 4.21: The figure shows the cumulative net injection across all the wells with the
minimum and maximum for each season indicated in red and green respectively. Addi­
tionally, the water production is shown for reference.
There may be noticeable water production in the shoulder season following the extraction
season. According to GSD, the mechanisms governing this period may, however, be
different from those in the extraction season. This could include pockets of gas forming
in the reservoir due to the alternating flow.

4.7
Inherent difficulties in modelling the WPR
As described in Section 4.5.3, WPR is measured using the opening times of the automatic
valve, which is only triggered when the water level reaches the LSH sensor. In other
words, only the integrated WPR is measured. These integrated measurements occur
when the cumulative water production since the last non­zero measurements reaches the
limit that triggers the automatic valve. Therefore, the actual water production may have
occurred during any of the zero measurements leading up to a non­zero measurement of
WPR. Hence, the uncertainty of WPR in a sample due to the way it was measured, is given
by the amount of water between the LSL and the LSH sensors, which corresponds to one
emptying of the FWS. This is assumed to be the value 0.972 m3/h equivalent to 81 l, which
provides an upper bound for the uncertainty of all samples. This value, which gives an
absolute uncertainty in WPR, may have a slightly different value depending on the volume
of water between the LSL and LSH sensors, however, we consider the arguments in this
section to be valid regardless of its precise value.
As the WPR is only sampled when a specific threshold of water is reached in the FWS,
this may be viewed as an unevenly spaced time series. This way, all the observations
with a water production rate of 0 m3/h can be regarded as missing samples.
The uncertainty and uneven spacing of the WPR measurements might influence the per­
formance of our time series models negatively. Therefore, it would be beneficial if the
impact could be reduced. One way of achieving this could be to transform these dis­
crete measurements into samples that mirror the actual water production. However, this
requires knowledge of how the true water production behaves. If the actual water pro­
duction was consistent and continuous, an interpolation and rescaling could be applied
easily, but this is not the case. According to GSD, the water is primarily produced in slugs,
which is a flow pattern known to also exist in oil wells, (Ahmed 2019, [4]). These slugs of
water may be extracted at any time instance preceding the measured water production,
44
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:55==
4.7. Inherent difficulties in modelling the WPR
and therefore, the measured WPR cannot easily be transformed to the actual WPR. The
notion of slug flow is supported by Figure 4.9 in Section 4.5.3, where the measured WPR
is seen to vary in both value and spacing in a period with continuous GPR and substantial
water production. If the true water production was continuous, measurements that were
both more evenly spaced and more similar in value would be expected. The magnitude
of several of the measurements is above 2 m3/h. which is more than one emptying of
FWS. Hence these measurements above 2 m3/h have produced at least 1 m3/h during
their 5­minute interval if the assumption about volume between the two sensors holds.
Figure 4.24: The plots show the water production per sample for the well ST­11 in early
2019. The plot on the right is a slice from the plot on the left where only the 5­minute data
is shown.
Apart from interpolation, subsampling is a method that may be used to reduce the effects
of the uneven spacing and the uncertainty of the measurements. The uncertainty of 81 l is
unaffected by the volume of water produced in each sample, and therefore, subsampling
is used in this thesis. The effect of a reduced sample rate is seen in Figure 4.24, which
shows the water production per sample for both day and 5­minute samples for ST­11. The
water production is computed as the WPR times the interval between samples in hours.
For the 5­minute data, it is seen that the uncertainty interval around zero encapsulates
some of the non­zero measurements, and even the largest non­zero measurements just
barely escape this uncertainty interval. This, however, is not true for the day data where
the water produced in many of the samples is somewhat larger than the uncertainty. The
period shown in the figure is from the second extraction season in the test set, which
contains relatively little water production, so the effect is even greater for many of the
other seasons. In effect, the lower sampling rate of the data has reduced the relative
uncertainty of the WPR for the day data. In the day data, the volume 81 l is equivalent to
a WPR of 0.001125 m3/h, which is lower than the WPR in much of the extraction season.
Other sample rates are possible, but averaging the samples from a day also have the
advantage of removing daily variations that would further complicate the modelling task.
Additionally, the specific resolution of day data is also a sensible data resolution for the
ARIMA model as described in Section 5.1.
Another possible way to mitigate the problems described in this section is to use an en­
coding of the data. An example of this could be to encode the water production using
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
45
==PAGE:56==
Chapter 4. Data
two values for each time step where one of these values represent the number of time
steps until the next opening of the FWS, while the other value holds the size of this com­
ing measurement. This specific encoding will not be suitable for the ARIMA and K`BK
models, but it may benefit the LSTM network. Due to the time constraints of this project,
we have not investigated how this or any other possible encoding of the data compares
with subsampling.
4.8
Training, validation and test set
As described in Section 3.6, consecutive splits were used to divide our data set into a
training and test set. For the LSTM model, the training set of the ARIMA and K`BK
models was split further into a training and a validation set. The splits were chosen based
on the extrema of the netINJ described in Section 4.6.1 to ensure a reasonable balance
of hot and cold winters in each split.
The first day of the test set is 20 May 2017 as this defines the beginning of the pure
injection season following the shoulder season of 2017. Hence, the test set includes the
four last seasons of the data set, which includes three hot and one cold extraction season.
This is not ideal as the cold winters are inadequately represented, which should be taken
into account when evaluating the models. Also, at the time this split was decided upon,
a low minimum in netINJ was expected to take place in early 2021. It is also seen that
the test set is rather large as it makes up about a third of the data, which was in order to
include the colder winter of 2018 in the test set.
To have both a cold and a hot winter in the validation set, the first day of the validation
set is chosen to be 20 May 2015. The earliest 5.5 seasons then account for the training
set for the LSTM model while the first 7.5 seasons are the training set for the ARIMA and
K`BK models. The data splits for the models are summarised in Table 4.25.

4.8.1
Data set shift of the data partitions
A change in the distributions of the data set partitions is called a data set shift (Quiñonero­
Candela et al. 2013, [54]). This may influence both the development and the evaluation
of the models. If the distribution of the training set differs from the remaining data, the
model weights will be tuned towards describing this distribution. If the distribution of the
validation set differs from the remaining data, the model hyperparameters will be tuned
towards this distribution. When evaluating the model on the various data partitions, we
will use the metric described in Section 3.7.1 to calculate the error of the predictions of the
WPRs on each data split. The distribution of the WPR in each data partition will therefore
influence the corresponding error in the evaluation.
Day data was used for the models for the reasons outlined in Section 4.7. Therefore, the
distributions were investigated using day data. The focus is on the water production as it
is the target variable, though data set shift in any of the variables used in the multivariate
models may impair their performance.

==PAGE:57==

measurements above 1.3 m3/h are shown at ”1.3+”. Please note the logarithmic scale on
the y­axis.

compared with the test set. Water production in the training data is also observed more

validation set has the highest relative water production for most of the rates, and thus, the

with the other partitions. Furthermore, the means and medians are also observed to be
smaller in magnitude for the test set. However, as the WPR is zero more than 90 % of the
time for all partitions, the models will all be tuned towards zero predictions. Therefore the
error of the evaluation metric on the test set is suspected to be lower than for the training

5
Methods
In this chapter, it is described, how the various models were implemented including the
procedures used to tune them for the data set. Due to the number of wells in the data set,
only a relevant subset of these are discussed for each model.
5.1
Time series data resolution and ARIMA predictions
Using the original 5­minute data in the ARIMA models could impose problems in addition
to the difficulties discussed in Section 4.7, because a 7­day forecast would then require a
2016­step prediction. Simply illustrated by the k­step forecast of an AR(1) model: ˆyt+k =
φk
1yt, it is seen that even a very large AR coefficients close to 1, would effectively be
reduced to zero when making 7­day forecasts with a 5­minute data. Therefore, a lower
resolution of data is used. Reducing the resolution to 6 hours between samples, would
still require a 28­step prediction for a 7­day forecast horizon, and the contribution from
an AR(1) coefficient of 0.9 would then be reduced to approximately 0.05. Therefore, a
resolution of day data is chosen. A slightly finer resolution would also be practical though
it would necessitate modelling daily variations, which would further complicate the model.
5.2
Selection of model order in the ARIMA models
A separate univariate ARIMA model was applied to the WPRs for each of the 14 wells
and the aggregated WPRs of the two zones. The orders of each individual ARIMA model
were selected in accordance with the Box­Jenkins method outlined in Section 3.2.3.
An example of the method applied to the well ST­17 is included in Appendix G.1. Here,
an ARIMA(1,0,1) model was chosen to be the model best­suited for describing the water
production of this well. One key point from this example was the extensive water produc­
tion in the last extraction season of the training set for ST­17. Because of this, the model
tried to fit a coefficient at lag 37, which only seemed suitable for describing the water pro­
duction in this extraction season. As this coefficient was unlikely to describe the overall
water production of ST­17, it was omitted.
The second key point from the example in Appendix G.1 was that the residuals of the
ARIMA model did not resemble white noise sufficiently. Therefore, transformations of
the WPR were investigated, but these did not improve upon the residual structure of the
model. The issue was likely a result of the many zero measurements as only 699 of the
2611 days in the training set have non­zero WPR. In other words, the water production
equals zero around 73% of the days in the training set for ST­17.
5.2.1
ARIMA models for the wells and zone data
The ARIMA models for all remaining wells and the two aggregated zones were estimated
using the Box­Jenkins procedure as shown for ST­17 in Appendix G.1. It should be noted
that the ARIMA models for most of the individual wells were harder to determine than for
ST­17, probably due to the longer periods without water production compared with ST­17.
Therefore, the results of the residual analyses from these models are also unsatisfac­
tory in the sense that the distributions do not resemble a normal distribution sufficiently.
Transformations of the WPR were not able to remedy this as was the case for ST­17.
Furthermore, the Ljung­box statistic often showed that only for the first few lags the null
hypothesis of no correlation was not rejected. In spite of these difficulties, the ARIMA

From Table 5.1, it is seen that the ARIMA models for the individual wells’ WPR exhibit
much variation in the AR­ and MA­orders, and one well (ST­11) was found to have a
differencing term. This variation is worth investigating further. Therefore, the log­likelihood
for every ARIMA(p, d, q) model with p = 0, 1, ..., 10 and q = 0, 1, ..., 10 for all the wells and
two zones have been estimated on the WPR data. The differencing was d = 0 for the
two zones and all wells except ST­11 where d = 1. The results from the four wells ST­11,
ST­12, ST­16 and ST­18 are visualised in Figure 5.2 with the likelihoods for the optimal
parameters shown in the heat maps. For each model a ”0” or a ”1” is displayed along with
the log­likelihood. For models with a ”1” at least one of the AR­coefficients are ą 1. The
log­likelihoods for all the wells and two zones are included in Appendix G.3.
It is known that of two nested models, the log­likelihood of the larger model should be
at least as large as that of the smaller model. Therefore, if these log­likelihood plots do
not display this feature, then `BK function in _ does not converge sufficiently to optimal
coefficient values for the parameters.
ST­11 was the only well where the structure of the ACF indicated that a differencing term
might be needed. The resulting log­likelihood plot for the differenced WPR of ST­11 is
shown in Figure 5.2a, where it is seen that the log­likelihood is generally growing with
increasing model orders. There might be a few sub­optimal solutions e.g. at AR(6)­AR(8)
and MA(1)­MA(2), however, the figure shows an overall congruence with the notion that
the log­likelihood should not decrease for larger models when compared with smaller,
nested ones.

For the well ST­12, an ARIMA(5,0,3) was identified with the Box­Jenkins method, which is

local minimum of the data. However, from the log­likelihood plot of ST­12 in Figure 5.2b,
it is seen that there is a significant increment in log­likelihood going from AR(4) to AR(5)
while there is little gain in increasing the order of the autoregressive part further, validating
the model from the Box­Jenkins method.
The log­likelihoods for the well ST­16 are shown in Figure 5.2c. Here, the log­likelihood
is seen to almost exclusively increase with model order as expected. Please that the
ARIMA(0,0,0) model, i.e.the mean model, has a very low log­likelihood at 5971, and this
square was given its own red colour to be able to examine the remaining models in the
heat map.
Lastly, the log­likelihoods for ST­18 are shown in Figure 5.2d, where the minimum log­
likelihood for the ARIMA(0,0,0) model was also given its own red colour. This plot displays
the most typical behaviour displayed by the various wells and zones. Here, it is seen that
increasing the model from an AR(0) to an AR(1) model results in a large increment in
the log­likelihood, but increasing the model order beyond AR(1) does not result in any
significant increase in log­likelihood. This indicates that many of the models simply use
an AR(1) term to forecast the water production, which is similar to the random walk model.
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
51
==PAGE:62==
Chapter 5. Methods
From the plots, it looks like better solutions in terms of higher log­likelihood could be
found for ST­11 and ST­16. However, in constructing the models, we also investigated
the significance of parameters as well as the BIC. The latter penalises larger models
such that a substantial increase in log­likelihood is needed in order to prefer a model with
more parameters. When combining this with the results from the residual analyses, the
visualisation of the log­likelihood did not justify a change of the model orders from those
identified using the Box­Jenkins methodology.
5.3
Rudimentary K`BK model
It is natural to consider multivariate extensions to the ARIMA model as the inclusion of
multiple parameters is likely to provide information suitable for forecasting the water pro­
duction, which in itself contains relatively few non­zero measurements. Therefore, we
developed a K`BK model based on the theory outlined in Section 3.2.5.
One approach is to use this multivariate model to forecast the water production of the
individual wells and zones using the other measurements belonging to that well or zone.
In this scenario, the VARIMA could potentially include the six time series: WPR, GPR,
GIR, THP, THT and netINJ. The inclusion of these variables may account for the discrete
nature of the WPR by modelling the interactions between the variables.
A second approach is to construct a VARIMA model for each of the two zones using all
data from its wells. The water production for all the wells belonging to the zone is then
forecasted using all other variables belonging to that zone. This path is most likely to
represent the underlying physics since wells in the same zone are connected to the same
underground reservoir, but it is also the most computationally expensive. Since the early
VARIMA models also produced lackluster forecasts as described in Section 6.2, this last
implementation approach was not investigated.
In the multivariate approach, it is possible to reduce the number of variables by not in­
cluding THT as it seems to contain little predictive power. Exclusion of this variable is
only expected to reduce the accuracy of the model slightly, if at all. Moreover, GPR and
GIR may be combined into one ”flow variable”, which can have both positive and negative
values. However, the combination of this to will cause GPR and GIR to carry the same
weight in the model which may be an oversimplification.
5.3.1
Differencing of the time series
Initially, we attempted to develop a K`BK model which included THP, GPR and netINJ
in addition to WPR. The variables THP and GPR were selected as they showed the great­
est correlation with WPR while netINJ was suggested by GSD based on past experience.
From a physical point of view, these three variables are also likely to be significant consid­
ering how the analytical models for water coning are usually based on the extraction rate
and the height of the gas layer in the underground among others. Both THP and netINJ
incorporate information about the level of gas in the reservoir.
The first step in establishing the K`BK model is to use suitable differencing. We fol­
lowed the approach outlined by Spliid 2016, [69], which is to use the order and lag of the
differencing operations from ARIMA models for the same variables. Therefore, we used
the differencing terms as they appear for the estimated models in Table 5.1 seen earlier.
They were determined using the Box­Jenkins method outlined in Section 3.2.3. One could
expect that yearly seasonal terms for some of the time series would be appropriate since
GSD has a seasonal approach to operating the facility as explained in the introduction,
however, we did not find that seasonal ARIMA coefficients benefited the ARIMA models.
52
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:63==
5.3. Rudimentary K`BK model
Initially, the intention was to make K`BK models for all the wells and zones with the
variables WPR, THP and GPR. Therefore ARIMA models were estimated for these vari­
ables. However, K`BK models were only developed for the two wells ST­11 and ST­17
along with the two zones for reasons discussed further in Section 6.2. Therefore, ARIMA
models for netINJ were only developed for these wells and the zones. The choice of
ST­11 and ST­17 was motivated both by the relatively large amount extraction as well as
the experience from the ARIMA models where they appeared to be easier to model than
the remaining wells. The zones were chosen because the time series for the aggregated
production variables are relatively smooth compared with those for the individual wells.
However, this aggregation of data may also obscure important features for the prediction
of the WPR.
After developing the ARIMA models, the differencing terms for each variable are known.
From these differencing orders, it became apparent that GPR and netINJ ended up being
very similar. Applying one order differencing to the netINJ, simply results in a subtraction
of GPR from GIR. As netINJ was differenced twice and GPR was differenced once, the
resulting time series contain very similar information. Therefore, it would be worth explor­
ing whether netINJ should be substituted for GIR or whether GPR should be excluded
altogether if this model framework was investigated further.
5.3.2
Cross­correlation of WPR and the included variables
Constructing a K`BK model based on the correlation of the residuals is likely to be even
more challenging than in the univariate case. For this reason, we have chosen to use
a backward model selection approach, where we apply a K`BK model to the data and
remove the insignificant coefficients based on the usual threshold p ă 0.05. The pro­
cedure of model fitting and removal of insignificant coefficients is then repeated until all
coefficients in the K`BK model are significant. This approach comes with its own set of
shortcomings, and a grid search over the parameters would have been a more robust but
also more computationally expensive method.
Initially, a large model order of 100 was chosen where the order refers to both the au­
toregressive and the moving average terms meaning that all coefficients up to 100 were
fitted. It is reasonable to expect the activity at the beginning of a production season to
influence the water production at a later stage, which this model order would more or less
capture. Naturally, the behaviour in the injection season may also have a large influence
on the water production but this would require an even larger model order to capture. This
model order of 100, however, proved to be too difficult to process computationally. As a
remedy, we tried to identify the lags from the included variables that might be influential
on WPR based on the pre­whitening procedure described in Section 3.3. The estimated
ARIMA models for THP, GPR and netINJ in Table 5.1 was used for pre­whitening, and
the cross­correlation with WPR was tested up to lag 400 to allow for the influence of the
injection season. Additionally, all model orders up to and including 7 were used as this
covers all of the ARIMA models listed in Table 5.1.
To illustrate the choice of lags included in the K`BK models, the cross­correlation be­
tween the THP and the WPR for ST­17 is visualised in Figure 5.3. The ARIMA(3,1,3)
model for the THP was developed using the Box­Jenkins method outlined in Section 3.2.3.
This model was used to pre­whiten both the THP and the WPR, and the residuals from
these models were used to estimate the cross­correlation between THP and WPR. In
Figure 5.3, the negative lags are where previous time steps of the THP shows correlation
with the current WPR, which is of interest in forecasting the WPR. The most significant
lags are in this case seen to occur at 37 and 379, which are then included in the K`BK

As illustrated in this section, many compromises were made in the modelling phase, and
the choices were guided by computational limitations and time constraints, which pre­
vented us from exploring the range of possible K`BK models in more detail. Therefore,
the resulting models are not optimal, and therefore, they should only be regarded as pro­
totype models.
As explained in Appendix G.1, the correlation at lag 37 for ST­17 appears because of the
two peaks at the end of the training set meaning that a model that includes this coefficient
is likely fitted to the noise in the training set. Therefore, a model without this coefficient
presumably generalise better and provides more reasonable forecasts. This was tested
by fitting two K`BK models for ST­17 where one included coefficients at lag 37 while the
other did not.
5.4
LSTM
In several studies including Loh et al. 2018, [44], Bao et al. 2020, [7] and Liu et al. 2020,
[41] accurate forecasting of oil and gas production was achieved with recurrent neural
networks using the LSTM block. As LSTM models have proven useful for describing
production patterns in the oil and gas industry, we consider it a natural approach for at­
tempting to forecast the WPR. Day data was used for the LSTM model as it was used for
the ARIMA and K`BK models.
54
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:65==
5.4. LSTM
Convolutional neural networks (CNN) were also reasonably popular in studies relating to
time series forecasting within the petroleum industry though they were most often part
of a hybrid network, which also had an RNN component, (Abdullayeva et al. 2019, [2],
Yuan et al. 2021, [85]). Because of their relatively lower popularity and the difficulties
we encountered when attempting to investigate spectrograms of the data as described
in Appendix D, we decided not to pursue a CNN but instead focus on an RNN using the
LSTM block. However, it is possible that a CNN would provide comparable or even better
forecasts than an RNN.
5.4.1
Overall architecture of the LSTM network
The overall architecture of the network used in this thesis was chosen based on the com­
monalities between the networks found in the literature as outlined Appendix J.1, but with
a focus on the network applied by Loh et al. 2018, [44]. The fundamental component
of the proposed network is the LSTM block described in Section 3.5.2. An illustration of
the overall architecture of the network can be seen in Figure 5.5. Here it is seen that
the LSTM blocks can be stacked vertically for a deeper network, and the data is inputted
sequentially to the LSTM block in the first layer. As encountered in the literature, the se­
quence inputted to the network in each training loop is shorter than the full time series,
which was done for computational purposes and to improve gradient flow. Please note
that the time ˜t refers to the time steps in each sequence inputted to the network and not
the overall time step in the full time series. The intention of the model was to generate a 7­
day forecast for the WPR following each input sequence. Therefore, the last hidden state
vector outputted from the LSTM part of the network was used as input to a fully connected
(FC) network, which produced the final 7­dimensional output of the model equivalent to a
7­day forecast. The network was developed in PyTorch and the code for the network can
be seen in Appendix J.2.

5.4.2
Multi­step forecasting and loss function
The stated goal of making forecasts seven days ahead is achieved by the 7­dimensional
output variable from the FC layers where each output corresponds to a forecast for well

This loss is used to evaluate the model during training. Adopting this loss function forces
the network to learn to output reasonable forecasts for each horizon up to and including
h = 7. However, it is possible that training the network on only one prediction horizon
would result in more accurate forecasts for the specific horizon. A separate network could
then be trained for each horizon if multi­day forecasting was desired.
Probabilistic forecast
The LSTM network should produce a probabilistic forecast. To achieve this, Monte Carlo
dropout is used during test time, which produces an ensemble of forecasts for each ob­
servation. The distribution of this ensemble can then be used to give a mean prediction
as well as a 95% prediction interval. These prediction intervals should be interpreted
with caution as their quality may suffer. The network is only trained using a loss function
which penalises the mean prediction instead of a distribution. Hence, the prediction inter­
vals estimate the uncertainty of the model itself and not the uncertainty of the predictions.
As a consequence, the prediction estimates might be underestimated using Monte Carlo
dropout. A better but more time­consuming estimate of the uncertainty would be to train
an ensemble of networks.
5.4.3
Hyperparameters
The LSTM network needs to be tailored to the data at hand to be capable of producing
good forecasts of the WPR. In addition to tuning weights as outlined in Section 3.5.3, this is
accomplished by adjusting the hyperparameters. Many of these hyperparameters control
the capacity of the network, which necessitates a validation set to assess the performance
of a set of values for the hyperparameters.
In this thesis, the hyperparameters were estimated using a grid search on different com­
binations. For each configuration, the weights were tuned using the Adam optimiser on
the training set, and the model was evaluated on the validation set using the data par­
titions defined in Section 4.8. Early stopping was used to select the optimal epoch as
training should cease when the validation error starts increasing to avoid overfitting. Ide­
ally, this procedure should estimate the minimum validation loss of each configuration and
the overall minimum may then be used to choose the best set of hyperparameters. It is
important to be aware of the many possible local minima that can obscure the picture.
Table 5.6 lists 16 different hyperparameters that may be estimated, which would result
in a very comprehensive grid search. Therefore, only four of these hyperparameters are
tuned within the grid search while the values for the remaining were chosen based on
findings in the literature and initial experiments. The central part of the network is the
LSTM blocks, and therefore, the three hyperparameters relating to these are all tuned in
the grid search. In addition to these, the FC dropout rate is also estimated within the grid
search for the probabilistic forecasting reasons outlined in Section 5.4.2.
The main task of the fully connected part of the network is to be a function mapping the last
hidden state to the 7­day forecast. No activation function is used on the output because
56
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:67==

the predicted variable, WPR, is continuous. At least two layers are needed to allow for
non­linear relationships. Using this insight along with what has been observed in the
literature, the fully connected network is simply decided to consist of two layers. First,
the hidden state vector is mapped to 10 neurons where batch normalisation is applied
before the ReLU activation function. Then the 10 neurons are mapped to the desired
7­dimensional output.
For the input hyperparameters, the sequence length is set to 730 days equivalent to two
years. The reason being that the LSTM network should have the option to learn the
long­term dependencies provided by the previous extraction season as well as from the
injection season leading up to the current extraction season. This sequence length en­
sures the presence of at least two extraction seasons in each sequence. The hidden
state and the cell state are both initialised to zero, which is also considered reasonable by
Graves 2012, [20]. There might be a performance gain from optimising the initialisation of
the states as these might stabilise more rapidly, but this would yield an extra set of hyper­
parameters. The weights are initialised using a uniform distribution based on the number
of hidden units in the hidden state, which was shown by He et al. 2015, [26] to benefit
convergence of a network compared with an initialisation using a Gaussian distribution.

.
Regularisation techniques can be used to control overfitting by reducing the model vari­
ance. Hence, we need enough regularisation to ensure that the training error is not well
below the validation error. Conversely, imposing too much regularisation can result in a
model with too much bias. Some regularisation is imposed by the dropout rates, but more
regularisation tools could be investigated.
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
57
==PAGE:68==
Chapter 5. Methods
The batch size is the number of sequences to be processed simultaneously. The network
is trained using mini­batch gradient descent since the batch size is smaller than the total
number of sequences. The batch size influences learning as a larger batch size provides
a more accurate and stable estimate of the gradient, but this also makes it harder to break
out of a potential local minimum. Smaller batch sizes add more noise to the gradients,
which make it easier to break out of a local minimum. The choice of batch size is also
influenced by the optimiser and the learning rate and after experiments, we settled on a
batch size of 256 and a learning rate of 0.0001. The Adam optimiser was chosen as it
is a popular choice that combines momentum and a normalisation of the step size. Its
parameters could be tuned, but the reasonable defaults mentioned in Section 3.5.3 are
used instead. Lastly, the number of epochs is chosen using early stopping, which requires
enough epochs to observe a minimum in the validation loss.
5.4.4
Network inputs and sanity check of the implementation
The multivariate time series of each well is split into sequences of 737 days including the
7 days used in the loss function. These sequences are shifted by one day relative to each
other, which results in around 1300 sequences in the training set for each well. Hence, the
sequences for each well will overlap and share many repetitions and similarities, which
might result in overfitting earlier in the training. The sequences are shuffled to break some
of the correlations and give a gradient that is representative of the overall gradient.
Each sequence consists of the six well variables defined in Section 4.5 in addition to
the aggregated netINJ of the zone that the well is perforated to. The inclusion of the zone
netINJ is motivated by the information it provides of the total amount of gas in the reservoir,
which is not necessarily reflected by the well’s own netINJ. Therefore, the dimension of
the input variable xt in Equation (3.15) at time t is #i+?nbBx2 ˆ 7.
Before the network architecture and methods described in this section was applied to
the data set, a proof of concept was performed to investigate whether the network was
capable of fitting to a synthetic data set. This was done by generating a synthetic well data
set that resembled the real data but with known interdependencies. In Appendix I, the data
generation procedure, as well as the training of the LSTM network, are described. The
network was capable of fitting to the data, and therefore, we proceeded with this model
implementation on the real data set.
5.4.5
Transfer learning and selection of hyperparameter
The goal is to make 7­day forecasts of WPR for each of the 14 individual wells. Hence,
a separate model could be developed for every well, which would include a separate grid
search to estimate the hyperparameters. This would be a very time­consuming task. In­
stead, one model was developed for the whole facility and the hyperparameters were
estimated for all the wells simultaneously. Hence, each batch supplied to this model con­
sists of sequences randomly chosen in both starting times and among the wells.
This procedure creates a ”field” network, which has some advantages. First of all, using
the field network to estimate a common set of hyperparameters reduces the complexity
of the task. Furthermore, the size of the data set also increases from around 1300 se­
quences for each well to around 18200 sequences for the full data set. This supplies
the network with more information that may enable the network to learn the average and
shared features across the facility.
Based on domain knowledge and previous analysis, differences between the wells are
known to exist (Section 4.4). Therefore, the field model might be sub­optimal for forecast­
ing all the individual WPRs compared with a model for each well. This was remedied by
58
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:69==
5.4. LSTM
using the field model as a starting point for further training on the data for each each in­
dividual well resulting in 14 models with the same hyperparameters but different weights.
Hence, besides using the field network for estimating the hyperparameters, it is also used
as a pre­trained network for the individual wells in the spirit of transfer learning. The devel­
oped field network model may potentially contain knowledge that would not be learnable
only using the data from the individual well. Hopefully, this knowledge is transferred by
using the weights of the field model as a starting point.
Grid search for the field network
Table 5.7: Sets of hyperparameters used in the grid search.

As mentioned in Section 5.4.3, the hyperparameters were estimated using a grid search
for the field network. The examined set of hyperparameters can be seen in Table 5.7, and
these were chosen based both on initial experimental results and the hyperparameters
used literature, (Loh et al. 2018, [44], Bao et al. 2020, [7], Liu et al. 2020, [41]). Please
note that the LSTM dropout rate is the dropout rate between LSTM layers, which can only
be greater than zero, when there is more than one layer. Furthermore, the number of
hidden units (size of the hidden and cell states) was identical across the LSTM layers.
Therefore, a total of 117 combinations of hyperparameters were investigated.
Figure 5.8: The heat map shows the sample means of the minimum validation MSE for
three runs from the grid search over the hyperparameters. The numbers in the heat map
are the mean of the minimum validation MSE ˆ104.

==PAGE:70==
Chapter 5. Methods
To mitigate the effect of local minima, three initialisations were tried for each combination
of the hyperparameters. The exception to this was the sets that included one layer, since
the optimal sets of hyperparameters seemed to include only one LSTM block. For these,
an additional six initialisations were completed to better estimate the minimum validation
error. The minimum validation MSE for each of the initialisations was stored, and the
means of these are shown in Figure 5.8, where each tile corresponds to a different com­
bination of hyperparameters. The numbers reported in the heat map are the mean of the
minimum validation MSE ˆ104. There were three combinations of hyperparameters with
a very similar minimum mean validation MSE around 0.267.
Figure 5.9: The heat map shows the lengths from the means to the ends of the confidence
interval for the minimum validation MSE, which has been calculated from a t­distribution.
The numbers in the heat map are ˆ104 to make the scaling identical to that of Figure 5.8
95 % confidence intervals for the minimum validation errors were computed using a t­
distribution and an unbiased estimate of the standard deviation, which was necessary
due to the small number of samples. The lengths from the sample means to the ends
of these confidence intervals are shown in Figure 5.9. Hence, the lower bounds of the
intervals are computed by subtracting the values in Figure 5.9 from the means shown in
Figure 5.8 and the upper bound are computed by adding the lengths to the means. Large
overlaps between the confidence intervals are visible, especially from the combinations
of hyperparameters in the bottom part of the plots. This is not surprising given the low
number of samples. For this reason, an optimal set of hyperparameters with a statistically
significant minimum validation MSE cannot be chosen, based on the completed initialisa­
tions. Therefore, the selection of hyperparameters was based on the overall minimum of
60
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:71==
5.4. LSTM
the means, which was obtained for the set of hyperparameters consisting of 1 layer, 20
hidden units, an FC dropout rate of 0.1 and no LSTM dropout. Furthermore, this set of
hyperparameters also has a relatively narrow confidence interval, and even though it is
not significantly better than many of the other combinations with 1 layer, it is significantly
different from some of the sets with 3 or 5 layers. A sketch of the network with this set of
hyperparameters is shown in Figure 5.10.

If time had allowed it, more combinations of hyperparameters could have been inves­
tigated and a finer grid of hyperparameters could have been used for subsequent grid
searches. Potentially, the optimal set of hyperparameters could then be chosen based
on statistical significance, instead of simply basing it on the minimum of the mean of the
validation MSEs.
5.4.6
Learning curves for the field model
For the chosen set of hyperparameters, the field model was trained three times with ran­
dom initialisation of the weights. The learning curves from these three initialisations are
shown in Figure 5.11, where the solid curves are the validation losses and the more faint
curves are the training losses. The minimum validation loss for each initialisation is indi­
cated by a dashed, vertical line. The MSE losses for the full training and validation split
are shown in the top figure, and the MSE for the non­zero WPR measurements in each
set are shown in the bottom figure. Please note that the best model is chosen based on
the minimum loss on the full validation set. However, the epochs with the minimum val­
idation loss for the full validation set coincide almost exactly with the epochs minimising
the validation loss for only the non­zero WPR measurements in the validation set. Hence,
the field models selected by the overall validation losses also nearly minimise the MSE
during periods of water production.
The magnitude of the training losses in Figure 5.11 is observed to be much lower than
those of the validation losses. This is undesired as it suggests that the network is too
complex and overfits to the training data which results in a poor performance on the val­
idation set. The relative difference between the training and validation losses is around
a factor of 70 when all measurements are considered, whereas the relative difference for
non­zero WPR is around a factor of 140. Hence, the models seem to generalise worse to
the periods of water production. This might be because the WPR consists of more than
90 % zero­measurements, which could favour zero­predictions resulting in a higher loss
for periods of water production.

Figure 5.11: Learning curves for the field model. The training curves are thinner and more
faint than the validation curves. The dashed lines indicate minimum validation loss.
Losses on each well for the field models

validation data from all 14 wells, and therefore, the performance on each well was inves­

each of the 14 wells and their combined (field) data set are shown in Table 5.12. In this
table, the range of the training losses across the wells is observed to be reasonably small.
Hence, the each model was fitted to a similar degree to each of the wells in the training
data. It can also be seen that the field models describe the training set of ST­16 as the
worst among all the wells. Initialisation 2 is seen to have the lowest training loss for all
the wells, so this model has fitted the most to the training data.

==PAGE:73==
5.4. LSTM
The validation losses of the individual wells in Table 5.12 reveal a large range of the mag­
nitudes across the wells. Therefore, the choice of optimal field models was not influenced
evenly by the wells’ validation sets. The largest validation losses for all initialisations are
observed for the two wells ST­16 and ST­17. Their validation losses are around 20 and 50
times larger than the third largest validation loss, which is from ST­18. Hence, the choice
of field models was mainly dictated by the validation sets from ST­16 and ST­17. This is
visible for initialisation 3, which has the lowest validation loss for the combined field data,
however, only ST­16 and ST­17 obtain the lowest validation loss at initialisation 3. The
rest of the wells obtain the lowest validation loss at initialisation 2, which is the field model
with the highest validation loss on the combined validation set and also the one with the
largest validation losses for ST­16 and ST­17. Therefore, this indicates that the second
field model generalises the best to the majority of wells at the cost of fitting the least to
the validation sets of ST­16 and ST­17.
Furthermore, in each initialisation, the newest wells (i.e. ST­16 to ST­20) are observed
to have a greater validation loss compared with their training loss. However, this trend
is reversed for the remaining wells. From this, it seems likely that there are some distri­
butional differences in the water production between the validation sets of the older wells
and those of the newest wells. This would also be in agreement with the grouping of
similar wells in Table 4.5 where the newest wells share a group.
Validation set WPR distribution of the wells
In Section 4.8.1, the aggregated WPR for all wells was seen to be somewhat evenly dis­
tributed between the training and validation set. However, the differences in the training
and validation losses for the individual wells indicate that there are differences across
the wells. Therefore, the distribution of the WPR in the validation set for the newest
wells are shown alongside the distribution of the WPR in the validation set of the re­
maining wells in Figure 5.13. In this figure, consistently more water production is ob­
served for the newest wells at all rates except for WPRs around 0.2 m3/h and, on average,
these wells produce 4 times more water in the validation set compared with the remain­
ing wells. Moreover, the average water production in the validation set for the newest
wells is twice as large compared with the water production in the combined training set.
This trend is reversed for the validation set of the remaining wells where a lower wa­
ter production is observed at almost all rates compared with the combined training set.
Furthermore, a comparison between the training and validation set for the newest wells
shows that the average WPR is almost eight times larger in the validation set. Similarly,
comparing the remaining wells’ validation set to their own training set shows that the
average WPR is around three times larger in the training set. The training set distributions
can be seen in Appendix J.3.
To recapitulate the findings, a relatively large water production is seen in the validation
set for the newest wells. This may explain their large contribution to validation losses of
the field model, which is mainly trained on samples with zero WPR.
The data set shifts seen in the distributions of WPR for the training and validation sets
introduce some difficulties. Firstly, if the training data does not represent the overall dis­
tribution of the data, then the weights cannot be tuned to reflect the overall distribution.
Secondly, if the validation data does not represent the overall distribution of the data,
then an optimal set of hyperparameters may not be chosen, and a sub­optimal model
might be selected based on the minimum validation loss. Lastly, any distributional differ­
ences between the training and validation sets of the wells influence the calculated losses.

Therefore, it becomes difficult to draw any definite conclusion of whether the model has
overfitted to the training training data or not based on the losses. Consequently, it may
not be known if the model could be improved by adjusting the regularisation. The im­
mediate conclusion to draw from the differences in the training and validation losses of
the learning curves in Figure 5.11 is that the field models has overfitted to the training
data. However, this conclusion is only valid if the training and validation sets reflect the
underlying distribution, which was seen to likely be wrong.
5.4.7
LSTM models for the wells
As mentioned in Section 5.4.5, the best field model from each of the three initialisations
was used as a pre­trained network for each individual well. This resulted in three learn­
ing curves for each well where the model minimising the validation error of the well was
chosen as the best model of the well. The learning curves for all wells can be seen in
Appendix J.4.
As an example of the model selection procedure, the learning curves for the well ST­11
are shown in Figure 5.14. Though noisy, the training loss is observed to decrease with
increasing epochs while the validation loss is seen to decrease initially before it starts
increasing. The minimum validation loss for each of the three initialisations occurs at the
epochs 1483, 2813 and 1708 respectively. The model with the overall lowest validation
loss, which has a minimum validation MSE of 1.15 ˆ 10´3 at epoch 2813, is the model
using the pre­trained field model 2 as a starting point. Therefore, this model was selected
for predicting the water production from ST­11.
The procedure described for ST­11 was used to select the best model for each well. How­
ever, for the wells ST­2, ST­7 and ST­13, the lowest validation losses occurred at dubious
downward spikes that needed further investigation. This is shown for ST­7 in Figure 5.15.
Here, it is observed that overall minimum validation loss happens at epoch 939 using
the first pre­trained field model. However, this minimum validation occurs as a sudden
downward spike after a long period of increasing validation loss. It is not easily seen from
the figure, but at this epoch, there is also a sudden increase in the training loss. Hence,
the gradient from the loss function has taken the model a step away from the minimum,
64
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:75==
5.4. LSTM
Figure 5.14: Learning curves for the well ST­11. The training curves are thinner and more
faint than the validation curves. The dashed lines indicate minimum validation loss.
Figure 5.15: Learning curves for the well ST­7. The training curves are thinner and more
faint than the validation curves. Please note the logarithmic scale on the y­axes.
which increased the training loss. Apparently, the model’s reduced fit to the training set
applies well to the validation set. Maybe this is because the number of zero predictions
has increased, which better describes the WPR distributions in the validation sets of the
older wells. However, the low validation loss of this epoch seems to occur by chance
when considering the remaining part of the curve. Therefore, the model obtaining the low
validation loss at epoch 939 from initialisation 1 was disregarded. Instead, the model with
the second­lowest validation loss, which appears at epoch 454 from initialisation 2, was
chosen. This minimum is also seen to follow a period of steadily decreasing validation
loss. Similarly, the wells ST­2 and ST­13 have their overall minimum validation losses fol­
lowing spikes in the validation loss, and therefore, these minimum validation losses were
disregarded, and other minima that follow a decreasing trend were chosen for ST­2 and
ST­13.

Following the above procedures, a model was identified for each well. These are reported
in Table 5.16 with their pre­trained network, the number of epochs used in training and the
resulting training and validation losses. The performance of the best field model measured
on the validation set of the same well is listed for comparison. It was observed that almost
all wells experienced a performance gain measured on the validation loss, and thus, they
benefited from training further on the individual training data. The only exception was
the well ST­16 where no gain was visible from further training. It was seen to reach the
lowest validation loss after one epoch, which is listed as epoch 0 in the table because of
zero­based indexing, and the validation loss was larger than the corresponding validation
loss for the raw field model. Hence, the field model from initialisation 3 was selected as
the best model for ST­16.

a better local minimum for these wells even though the initial weights from the pre­trained
field model 2 started at a lower validation loss. Many of the learning curves using the
pre­trained field model 2 only experience a slight reduction in validation loss, which may
be because the weights of this model were already tuned strongly towards a relatively
isolated local minimum of the training loss where only a slight reduction in the validation
loss was possible.
