6
Results
In this section, the results from the ARIMA, K`BK and LSTM models are discussed. Due
to the number of wells, only a relevant subset is discussed for each model framework to
avoid needless repetition. At the end of the section, a pairwise comparison of the residuals
from the models is discussed.
6.1
ARIMA
The RMSEs of each of the seven forecasting horizons were calculated for the various
ARIMA models describing the WPR for each well. The raw RMSEs for the wells and
the zones are reported in Appendix G.4 where it can be seen that RMSEs are generally
increasing for longer forecast horizons. In Table 6.1, the normalised RMSE for the 7­
day forecasts of each zone and well are shown. The RMSEs of the ARIMA models has
been divided by the RMSEs of the mean models as described in Section 3.7.1. The
mean models have the same RMSEs for all forecasting horizons. Therefore, the mean
models will have the largest probability of outperforming the ARIMA models for the 7­day
forecasts.

From Table 6.1, it is seen that the RMSEs for ST­2 and ST­7 on the whole training set are
almost numerically equal to those of the mean model. Similarly, ST­1, ST­8, ST­13, ST­14
and ST­20 all have normalised RMSEs very close to 1. The similarity in 7­day forecasts
to the mean models may be explained by the AR coefficients in the models. However,
contributions from the MA parts to the forecasts should not be neglected. It is known from
the model orders in Table 5.1 that the largest MA order of an ARIMA model is 4. Hence,
a prediction error at time t can have a direct influence on the predictions up to time t + 4.
However, this influence is dependant on the magnitude and sign of the prediction error,
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
67
==PAGE:78==
Chapter 6. Results
and therefore, the AR parts will describe the overall behaviour of the 7­day forecasts for
all the ARIMA models.
It is seen that two of the wells, ST­1 and ST­14, only have an AR­order of 1. The coef­
ficients of all the ARIMA models are listed in Appendix G.2. Inspecting the coefficients
of ST­1 and ST­14, ST­1 is observed to have the largest AR(1) coefficient of ´0.704, but
when making a 7­day forecast, then the coefficient will be raised to the power of 7, i.e.
0.7047 = 0.086. Therefore, hardly anything but the mean will be left for the 7­day fore­
casts. This is visualised in Figure 6.2, where the 1­day and 7­day forecasts of ST­1 in
the spring of 2013 are shown. For the 1­day forecasts, the AR(1) coefficient is seen to
result in predictions, where the WPR from the previous day is repeated with a reduction
in value. For the 7­day forecast in Figure 6.2, the predictions are almost reduced to the
mean value. Also, the delay in the forecasts is even more visible and severe here. The
95% prediction intervals naturally increase for the 7­day forecast, but they are seen to be
wide for periods with no water production while still failing to encapsulate WPR in periods
with water production.
Figure 6.2: Plots showing the forecasts of the ARIMA(1,0,2) model for ST­1 for the fourth
extraction season in the training set. The leftmost figure shows the 1­day forecasts while
the figure on the right shows the 7­day forecasts.
In Figure 6.2, the contribution from the two MA­parts is especially apparent at the end
of the time series around 1 May for the 1­day forecasts. Here, a large forecasting error
causes a decaying oscillation around zero for the subsequent 1­day forecasts.
The ARIMA models for the wells ST­2, ST­7, ST­8, ST­13 and ST­20, also with normalised
RMSE close to 1, have AR­orders in the range from 2 to 6. The contributions to the 7­day
forecasts from the coefficients cannot be interpreted as easily as for the ARIMA models
with an AR­order of 1. However, from a visual inspection of the forecasts, it seems that
7­day forecasts from these models are similar to those of the mean models. This is also
indicated by the normalised RMSEs in Table 6.1for the test set metrics, which are all close
to 1 for these wells.
We now turn our attention to the most promising ARIMA model in terms of having the
lowest normalised RSMEs for the 7­day forecasts. Omitting ST­9, which is not used on
a regular basis, the well with the lowest normalised RMSEs is ST­11. ST­11 is the only
well that ended up with a differencing term in the ARIMA model, therefore, its coefficients
describe the changes in the WPR for the time series. In Figure 6.3, the 1­day and 7­day
forecasts for the beginning of 2018 are shown. Here, the values of the 1­day forecasts are
seen to be closer to the last observed values compared with ST­1. Also, for the 7­day fore­
68
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:79==
6.1. ARIMA
Figure 6.3: Plots showing the forecasts from the ARIMA(1,1,2) model for ST­11 for the
first extraction season in the test set. The leftmost figure is the 1­day forecasts while the
figure on the right shows the 7­day forecasts.
cast, the predictions are not severely reduced towards the mean value. However, both
the 1­day and the 7­day forecasts are seen to simply be delayed and smoothed observa­
tions as was the case for ST­1. This is natural as the forecasts are based on observed
values of WPR and predictions errors from the recent past only, which makes it hard for
the ARIMA model to forecast the change in WPR before it happens. The 95% prediction
interval in Figure 6.3 is seen to encapsulate most of the observed water production for
both the 1­day and 7­day forecasts. However, when water production stops, it takes some
time for the 7­day forecasts to decrease to zero. This sudden drop is also caused by the
well being turned off and it is hard to imagine a model that is able to predict this.
Figure 6.4: Plots showing the forecasts from the ARIMA(1,1,2) model for ST­11 for the
fourth extraction season in the training set. The leftmost figure is the 1­day forecasts while
the figure on the right shows the 7­day forecasts.
Even though, it is observed in Figure 6.3 that the 95% prediction interval of the ARIMA
model’s 7­day forecasts encapsulates most of the observed water production in the ex­
traction season, this is not always the case. In the fourth extraction season in the training
set visualised in Figure 6.4, it is seen that most of the observations lie within the 95%
prediction interval for the 1­day forecasts. However, many water production observa­
tions lie outside the 95% prediction interval for the 7­day forecasts. Hence, even for the
best performing model, the ARIMA model is seemingly unable to describe the underly­
ing process of WPR during the extraction seasons. Furthermore, comparing the ARIMA
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
69
==PAGE:80==
Chapter 6. Results
model to the random walk model for ST­11, (Appendix F.1), we see that the ARIMA model
has lower normalised RMSEs on all training set metrics, where the random walk model
has lower normalised RMSEs on all test set metrics. While we cannot draw conclusions
based on the two models’ normalised RMSE, this simple comparison indicates that the
ARIMA(1,1,2) model is not better suited for modelling the WPR of ST­11 than the random
walk model.
Apart from ST­11, the ARIMA models for the wells ST­12 and ST­19 have the lowest
normalised RMSEs. These models are the second and third best performing measured
on this metric. However, both models fail to cover most of the observations within the 95%
prediction interval of the 1­day forecasts during extraction seasons, which can be seen in
Appendix G.5.
For the majority of the training and test set metrics, the RMSEs for the ARIMA models
are equal to or less than the mean models’ RMSEs, but the results shown in this section
implies that none of the ARIMA models was able to describe the underlying process of the
water production for any of the wells. We also had a hypothesis that the aggregated zone
data could be better suited for the ARIMA model. However, the normalised RMSEs for
the zones is not smaller than the best performing wells in each of the zones. The forecast
of the zones also suffers from the same issues as discussed in the above. Forecast for
the two zones can be seen in Appendix G.6.
There could be several explanations for why the ARIMA models generally have a hard time
capturing the nature of these time series. First of all, the ARIMA models do not include
exogenous variables and this lack of interaction between the variables disregards much
information. Another issue is that ARIMA models generally have difficulty in forecasting
outliers and some instances of water production may be considered outliers relative to the
rest of the data since they appear as spikes from a time series that is zero for long periods
of time. This is another facet of the problem that the WPR time series are zero for the ma­
jority of the days. Additionally, the nature of this forecasting problem is likely to be highly
non­linear, which is supported by the theory of water coning outlined in Section 3.1.1.
6.2
Rudimentary K`BK model
Prototype models based on the K`BK framework were developed for ST­11, ST­17, Zone
1­3 and Zone 5. The model building procedure is described in Section 5.3. The resulting
non­zero coefficients in the K`BK model for ST­11 are visualised in Figure 6.5. Similar
plots for the remaining K`BK models are included in Appendix H.2. The figures are
organised such that each 4 ˆ 4 matrix in the top row corresponds to the matrix at lag i for
the autoregressive part given in Equation (3.7). The diagonal elements then describe the
influence between lagged instances of the same variable. Similarly, the 4 ˆ 4 matrices
in the bottom row are the coefficients for the moving average parts, i.e the matrix given
in Equation (3.8). The x­axis denotes the lags and the variable names are indicated on
the y­axis. As explained in Section 3.2.5, negative AR coefficients translate to positive
correlation and the coefficients that affect WPR are in the rows labelled WPR.
From Figure 6.5, it is seen that relatively few variables influence the WPR. For the AR­
part, the only ones are GPR at lag 63 and 71. For the MA­part, GPR influence WPR at
lag 333, while THP affects WPR at lag 49 and 63. The variables may, however, have an
indirect influence on the WPR through the other cross­relations in the model. This pattern
of relatively few coefficients of significance to the WPR is also exhibited by the rest of
models, whose coefficients are visualised in Appendix H.2. It is worth remembering that
WPR for ST­11 has been differenced once, which may explain why there is no AR(1)
70
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:81==
6.2. Rudimentary K`BK model
Figure 6.5: The figure shows the non­zero coefficients in the K`BK model for ST­11 with
the AR­terms in the top rows and the MA­terms in the bottom rows.
coefficient in the first diagonal element at lag 1. It is also interesting to note that all of the
models find correlations between GPR and netINJ.
From Figure 6.5, it is also seen that the coefficients relating to the GPR are in general
numerically larger compared with the rest. This is explained by the slight differences in
scale for the variables, where the spread, computed as the difference between the largest
and the smallest value, is about 55 times greater for the differenced GPR on training set
compared with the one for the differenced WPR. The variables could be scaled prior to
fitting the model, however, this scaling should depend on the differenced variables and
the mean is already removed by the differencing operation. Running a small experiment
for ST­11, we found that it did not change the forecast errors as the scaling was merely
absorbed by the coefficients, which is also consistent with the regression method used to
determine the parameters in the K`BK­package in _, (Spliid 2016, [69]).
Figure 6.6: The figure shows the non­zero coefficients of the K`BK model for ST­17
without coefficients at lag 37.
Most of the K`BK models had at least one significant coefficient in each of the lags
given in Table 5.4. However, as seen in Figure 6.6, the model for ST­17 without lag 37
had no coefficient in lag 172. This shows that the K`BK model was not always able to
extract useful information from the lags, which also demonstrates that identifying important
lags for forecasting WPR using pre­whitening and cross­correlation is far from ideal. The
unsatisfactory performance of this method may be because the ARIMA models used for
the pre­whitening procedure did not result in white­noise residuals.
Table 6.7 shows the normalised RMSEs for the 7­day forecast, while the raw RMSEs
for all prediction horizons are listed in Appendix H.3. It is seen that ST­11 is the only
model that consistently outperforms the mean model. In contrast, the model for ST­17 with
coefficients at lag 37 performs significantly worse on the test set compared with the mean
model and an improvement is observed by not including lag 37. Furthermore, the error
from Zone 1­3, which includes the wells ST­1, ST­9, ST­11 and ST­12, is larger across the
board compared with the model for ST­11 alone. Therefore, it seems that even though the
averaging procedure made the variables more continuous, it also washed out some of the

as seen in Table 6.7. The forecasting trends are exemplified in Figure 6.8, which shows
7­day forecasts for the last extraction season in the training set and the first extraction
season of the test set. It is seen that the 95 % prediction interval is relatively large as the
interval around zero manages to encapsulate much of the water production. From the two
extraction seasons, it is evident that the model merely repeats recent water production.
This is also true for the black points, which the model has already seen. Therefore, it is
unreasonable to expect it to accurately forecast the water production for unknown data.

during water production. However, this is not necessarily an indication that the K`BK
model was able to describe the underlying process as the random walk model can be

6.2. Rudimentary K`BK model
seen to have an almost equally low error in Appendix F.1, which also supports the notion
that the forecasts from the K`BK model are similar to that of the naïve random walk.
Therefore, it is likely that this low RMSE is a reflection of the steady nature of WPR in
ST­11 which makes a relatively simple repetition of past measurements work reasonably
well.
Figure 6.9: Forecasts of the two K`BK models for ST­17. The forecasts from the model
with coefficients at lag 37 are in the top.
In Figure 6.9, the forecasts from the two K`BK models for ST­17 have been investigated
for two time periods. From the two leftmost plots, which shows 1­step forecasts for the
last extraction season in the training set, it is seen that the model with coefficients at lag
37 is highly tuned to forecasting the second peak. However, this results in some very
negative predictions on the test set as seen in the topmost plots. In contrast, the model
without the coefficient at lag 37 makes less unreasonable forecasts on the test set, though
it still fails to forecast the water production where it merely repeats a smoothed version of
the past observations. Of course, negative predictions of the WPR could be avoided by
setting these to zero. This would remove some of the worst forecasts and also improve
the RMSE but it would also somewhat obscure the behaviour of the model.
6.2.1
Potential further development of the K`BK model
If we were to explore these multivariate K`BK models further, a number of improvements
could be made. A more rigorous determination of lags that are likely to be influential would
be an enhancement. Preferably, an exhaustive parameter search would be used though
this seems to be out of the question due to computational limitations. It is, however, doubt­
ful whether simply including extra parameters would ameliorate the problems of the model
as it may still simply repeat the most recent observations. Moreover, including extra lags
might not result in more significant parameters as many coefficients were already zero for
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
73
==PAGE:84==
Chapter 6. Results
the included lags. Another path to explore is the inclusion of features from multiple wells in
one model since the wells are not independent. Lastly, both the ARIMA and K`BK model
could possibly benefit from modifying the time series by removing the injection seasons
which would also bring the residuals closer to the normality assumption.
As discussed in this section, these early results for the K`BK models are not promising
because of the relatively large errors and the fact that models are seen to merely repeat
the most recent observations. Moreover, the developed K`BK models did not show
consistent improvement over the ARIMA models. This, we attribute mainly to the difficulty
of the problem that may be highly non­linear, which is also supported by the approximate
analytical models for water coning. The non­linearities may be difficult to capture using
these K`BK models and it would probably require very time­consuming tuning that could
include manual feature engineering. Moreover, the seasonality does not appear to be
related to a specific time lag, and these multivariate ARIMA models are fundamentally
based on indices of time series, which makes them less well­suited for this type of task.
Taking all of this into account, our assessment is therefore that it would be difficult to adapt
these models to the problem at hand. For these reasons, we decided not to pursue this
line of modelling further and instead opted to use models with a greater capacity where
better forecasts may be achieved with less manual tuning.

6.3. LSTM model
From the training errors in Table 6.10, it is seen that around half of these are larger than
1 meaning that the networks performed worse on these wells compared with the mean
model using this metric. Please note that these training errors are not simply a scaling of
the training loss as the latter is evaluated continuously during training.
For some of the models, the errors on the training set are larger than the corresponding
errors on the validation and test sets as seen in Table 6.10. These include the models
for the wells ST­1, ST­9, ST­2, ST­7, ST­18, ST­19 and ST­20. This behaviour may be
attributed to the data set shift mentioned in Section 4.8.1.
The models for the wells ST­11, ST­12, ST­13, ST­14 and ST­17 all have relatively low
normalised training errors on all three metrics, meaning that these networks were able the
learn features useful for predicting the water production. However, for all of these models,
the errors on the validation set are seen to be somewhat larger, which could imply that
these features are not useful for predicting the water production in the validation set.
In other words, the models may have fitted to the noise in the training data, which is a
sign of poor generalisation. Although, it should be noted that the validation error is not
appropriate for judging generalisation as the hyperparameters have been tuned on this
split. However, the lacklustre generalisation of most of the models mentioned above is
confirmed by the test errors that are generally not much lower than those of the mean
models. The exception to this trend is the model for ST­11, which also has a relatively low
normalised test error on all metrics.
6.3.1
The models for ST­11 and ST­12
As seen from Table 6.10, the model for ST­11 seems to generalise the best. In fact, the
normalised error on the test for the samples with water production is even lower than
its counterpart on the training set. However, the errors on the validation set are still quite
large compared with those on the training set. The 7­day forecasts from this model for the
two first extraction seasons in the test set are seen in Figure 6.11. Please, note the smaller
scale of the plot on the right. From the plot on the left, many of the observations are seen
to lie outside the 95 % prediction interval. This demonstrates prediction intervals, which
were estimated using dropout, are too narrow at least in the extraction season. However,
the behaviour of these prediction intervals is reasonable since they shrink in periods with
no water production and expand when the forecasts start to increase in value.
Figure 6.11: 7­day forecasts of the LSTM model for ST­11 in the first and second extraction
season of the test set along with THP and netINJ from the well delayed by seven days.
In the season on the left in Figure 6.11, the water production was relatively large and the
network is seen to undershoot the peak WPR for this season somewhat. Conversely, for
the second season seen on the right, where the water production was relatively minor,
the peak water production is overestimated by the model. This seems to suggest that
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
75
==PAGE:86==
Chapter 6. Results
the network has learned to predict a somewhat large water production in the extraction
season regardless of whether sizeable water production has already occurred. This may
be viewed as an improvement over the ARIMA and K`BK models where both models
were seen to only repeat observations in a smoothed fashion.
In Figure 6.11, the lagged pressure is shown on an inverted axis. Both the pressure and
the netINJ for the well have been delayed by seven days such that these observations are
aligned with their resulting predictions. It is seen that the trajectory of the forecasts closely
resemble that of the THP in periods where the predictions are somewhat larger than zero.
Moreover, when the forecasts are close to zero, they seem to be much less affected by
fluctuations in the pressure. From this, it seems that the network has learned at least
two different states, one of which encodes sizeable water production. This is in contrast
to the coefficients found by the K`BK model for ST­11 presented in Figure 6.5. Here,
THP does not influence WPR through any AR coefficients, which is probably due to the
K`BK model not being able to encode the stateful nature of the problem as these direct
relationships only seem to be present during periods of substantial water production.
This behaviour could be investigated further by tracking the cell state with the aim of identi­
fying any changeover that occurs simultaneously with a rise in predicted water production.
The cell state is a vector of size 20, so any shift might not be immediately obvious. If al­
terations of the cell state could be identified, it would be interesting to investigate which
of the variables are important for the change to the cell state. It is conceivable that the
potential shift is caused by a long period with no observed water production, which the
LSTM cell has the potential to keep track of, but it may also be triggered by one of the
other variables. In the case of the latter, it is possible that the network uses a combina­
tion of the flow variable (GPR, GIR, netINJ) to make the shift in cell state. In this case,
removing these from the input would likely result in a different cell state and consequently
inferior predictions. However, from Figure 6.11, it is seen that network predicts substantial
water in periods where GPR is zero as visible by the flat nejINJ. Therefore, some impor­
tant connections with GPR are missing, which makes it possible the network only utilise
the flow variables to a small extent and any change to cell state may be a result of the
pressure reaching a threshold.
One relatively minor change that might compel the network to use a relationship between
GPR and WPR, would be to encode the loss such that only samples in a neighbourhood
around non­zero GPR measurements are used. This way, wrong predictions are only pe­
nalised when gas extraction takes place. This could also be interesting from a production
standpoint as GSD already knows that water is not produced when GPR is zero.
Figure 6.12: 7­day forecasts of the LSTM model for ST­12 in the first and second extrac­
tion season of the test set along with the lagged THP and lagged netINJ.
76
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:87==
6.3. LSTM model
Though the normalised test errors for ST­12 are not impressive when looking at the full
test set, its model has a somewhat low error when only considering observations with
non­zero WPRs. The fluctuations of the 7­day forecasts seen in Figure 6.12 look similar
to the ones exhibited by the pressure. However, unlike for ST­11, a sharp decline in the
WPR is observed without a similar drop in the pressure shortly after the major part of the
water production has occurred. This quick decay of the predictions, which may be the
result of zero gas flow, is impressive, but not that interesting to learn from a production
standpoint. It may however indicate that the model uses more variables than the THP.
Another remarkable point is that the model is seen to ignore the sudden spikes in water
production late in the extraction season that are visible in the plot on the left in Figure 6.12.
As seen from the netINJ, these are generated in a period where the well is dormant except
for the days where these peaks occur. As the network does not know when GSD is going
to start up a well, it has no way of predicting these peaks. For this reason, it is also
desirable that the model does interpret these as an indication of further water production.
The reason for this unresponsive behaviour might be found in the cell state as it is possible
that the network has switched to the state where it predicts almost no water causing it to
all but ignore the peaks. However, it is also possible that the network largely ignores the
current WPR when making predictions. One way to supply the network with information
that could be used to forecast these peaks would be to introduce a variable that encoded
expected gas production such that the model could know when the well was going to be
turned on. This could be investigated by including a lagged GPR in the training.
6.3.2
The models for ST­16 and ST­17
At first sight, some of the normalised errors in Table 6.10 seem characteristic to overfitting
where the training error is much lower than both the validation and test error. One of
these is ST­17, where the normalised training error on the full training set is low, while
the normalised validation errors are all close to 1. However, the training error for the
samples with a non­zero WPR is somewhat close to the mean model with a normalised
RMSE of 0.959. For ST­17, the validation set includes excessive water production while
the training set does not as noted in Appendix G.1. This skews the mean model as seen
in Figure 6.13a. Therefore, the low normalised RMSE for the full training set is partly a
result of a skewed mean model that always predicts a relatively large water production.
(a) Predictions from the LSTM model for ST­17
along with the mean on both the training set, and
the combined training and validation set.
(b) 7­day forecasts of the LSTM model for ST­16
for the training and validation set. Please note
the logarithmic scale for the WPR.
Figure 6.13: 7­day forecasts of the LSTM models for ST­17 and ST­16
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
77
==PAGE:88==
Chapter 6. Results
As seen in Figure 6.13b, relatively little water is produced in the training set for ST­16
while there is a major peak in 2017, which is part of the validation set as described in
Section 5.4.6. Therefore, these validation sets with excessive water production are likely
to favour models that predict much larger WPRs than what they saw in the training set.
This may be viewed as a form of extrapolation and this is unlikely to result in good gener­
alisation. The same issue was also encountered for the ARIMA and K`BK models.
The outcome illustrated above is just one more aspect of the data set shift outlined in
Section 4.8.1. It is important to note that removal of the seasons with excessive water
production is not a viable solution. The reason being that these are the seasons where
the water production represents the greatest potential issue which makes them desirable
to model. Also, when the measurements from a well only contain one of these ’extreme’
seasons, it is impossible to make a good split which in essence require repetitions.
Further to the model for ST­16, it was the only well for which the field model was used. In
Table 6.10, it is seen to have similar performance on all three splits where it only performs
slightly better than the mean model. This may in general be indicative of a model where
the bias is too large, which warrants an increase in model complexity. However, due to
the data set shift, it is not possible to make this direct connection. This issue becomes
obvious when inspecting the raw RMSE for ST­16 in Table J.25, in Appendix J.5, where
the validation RMSE is 22 times larger than the training RMSE.
The conservative nature of the model is also visible from Figure 6.13b, where the forecasts
seem to all stay close to zero. As mentioned earlier using different loss function might
change this tendency.
6.3.3
The models for ST­13 and ST­14
As was the case for ST­17, the training errors of the model for ST­13 and ST­14 are low in
comparison with the validation and test errors as seen from Table 6.10. The models and
the WPRs behave similarly for these two wells, whose perforations in the reservoir are
also situated relatively close to each other. Both of these wells have relatively few days
with non­zero water production, which makes it easier for the neural network to memorise
the whole sequence in the training set.
Figure 6.14: 7­day forecasts from the LSTM model for ST­13 for an extraction season in
the training set on the left and an extraction season in test set on the right.
The leftmost plot in Figure 6.14 shows the 7­day forecast for one of the few peaks in the
training set belonging to ST­13. It is observed that the LSTM network has fitted almost
perfectly to this spike, which is suggestive of a large degree of overfitting. Furthermore,
the shape of the peak seems almost identical to the peak in THP, which is reasonable
as the forecasts for ST­11 and ST­12 also seemed to depend heavily on the trajectory of
78
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:89==
6.3. LSTM model
the pressure. It is worth remembering that the THP and netINJ in the figure have been
delayed by seven days relative to the water production.
The rightmost plot in Figure 6.14 shows forecasts for ST­13 on an extraction season in
the test set. Here, the model does not capture the multi­day peak in WPR at all. This lack
of sensible forecasts on the test set also supports the notion that the model has overfitted
to the training data. Judging by the trajectory of the delayed THP and netINJ, there does
not seem to be much useful information available to forecast the WPR. It also appears
that the peak results in an increase in the forecasted WPR about one week after the spike
has died out, but this may also be due to the THP.
6.3.4
The behaviour of the state for ST­1
The leftmost plot in Figure 6.15 shows 7­day forecasts for ST­1. In the wake of the first
extraction season in the test set, very large prediction intervals surrounding zero forecasts
are observed. This continues until the large spike in the last extraction season is reached.
The mean of the l1­norm for the cell state, which is shown on the right in Figure 6.15, is
seen to hover around 2.0 for this entire period while it does not exceed 0.5 for the rest of
the data set.
Figure 6.15: The figure on the left shows 7­day forecasts from the LSTM model for ST­1
while the rightmost figure shows the mean of the l1­norm for the cell state at the time of
the 7­day forecast.
From Equation (3.15) in Section 3.5.2, the new cell state in each time step is given as
ct = ft d ct´1 + it d gt. Except for the previous cell state, each element in all vectors
is bounded above by 1 due to the activation functions, which aligns with the observed
values of the cell state around two. However, it may not be known which of the gates are
responsible for the value of the cell state by simply observing the average magnitude of
its elements.
By lowering the value of the spike in WPR that seems to cause this behaviour, this large
value of the cell state was avoided and forecasts more typical to the LSTM models were
observed for ST­1. Therefore, it seems that abnormal forecasts were triggered by this
spike in water production even though larger peaks were observed in the training set.
This hints at a general problem in training these neural network models since they require
training data that covers possible scenarios. The number of samples in the data set is
severely reduced if samples with zero WPR are regarded as missing. Additionally, the
data set only contains a limited number of extraction seasons and it may be reasonable
to consider each season as one instance of the water coning phenomenon as it manifests
itself at Stenlille. From this perspective, the size of the data set is fairly limited which
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
79
==PAGE:90==
Chapter 6. Results
makes it probable that future observations exhibit trends not found in the training set.
This makes the behaviour of the model unpredictable.
If the data set is truly too small to build a purely data­driven model that performs well, a
remedy is to use a model where knowledge about the physical behaviour may be included.
A good candidate for the problem at hand is a state space model. However, applying this
method successfully might require some knowledge of the underlying physics to make
reasonable assumptions about the parameters and distributions in the model. Also, in
the light of the recent experiment mentioned briefly in Section 1.2, it is possible that water
coning only plays a minor role.
6.3.5
Use of the field model
When comparing the normalised RMSE in Table 6.10 with the similar ones for the field
model in Table J.30 from Appendix J.6, it is seen that the training error generally decreases
as expected. However, the error on the full test set is also seen to decrease for 11 out of
the total 14 wells, which shows that the additional training produces models that generalise
better to the relevant well compared with the model for the whole facility.
Naturally, it would be interesting to compare this approach to a model trained on the
individual well only. However, to allow for a fair comparison the hyperparameters need to
be tuned equally well, which required cross validation on the individual well for the field
model. As mentioned in Section 5.4, this has not been done due to the resulting increase
in computational complexity.
From the discussion of the LSTM models for ST­11 and ST­12, it seems possible that a
relatively simple model with a few states and a scaling of the THP produces somewhat rea­
sonably but not spectacular predictions for these particular wells. However, this approach
does not appear to apply equally well to the remaining wells, which might be because the
underlying phenomenon that results in the water production is more complex.
6.4
Statistical comparison
The performance of all models on the test sets is compared using the difference in the
squared residuals as outlined in Section 3.9. In Table 6.16, the performance of the ARIMA,
mean and LSTM models evaluated on the full test set are compared to each other. In
Table 6.17 the performance of these three models are compared to the K`BK model for
the wells and zones the models have in common, the rest are marked by a ”´”. Please
note that the ∆ˆε2 in the tables are the difference in the squared residuals of the compared
models, e.g. ˆε2
ARIMA ´ ˆε2
mean.
Of the 57 pairwise statistical comparisons presented in the two tables, 33 yield a significant
difference in the performance of the compared models using a p­value threshold of 0.05.
The pFDR, (Section 3.9.1), for these 33 rejected null hypotheses is 0.0044, which means
that 0.1458 is the total number of falsely rejected null hypotheses. Hence, the following
examination of the statistical model performance comparisons is not severely affected by
positive false discoveries.
From Table 6.16 and Table 6.17, it is observed that the none of the developed models
shows significantly better performance than the mean model on the wells in Zone 5 except
for the LSTM model for ST­19. Furthermore, the LSTM model only outperforms the mean
model for ST­1 in Zone 1­3, whereas the ARIMA model outperforms the mean model on
three out of the four wells in Zone 1­3. Hence, when considering the squared residuals
on the test set, the mean model is hard to surpass for the models developed in this thesis.

Compared with the ARIMA models, the LSTM models are seen to be better for three of the
wells, while the ARIMA models are observed to have lower errors for five wells including
ST­11 and ST­12. The K`BK model is observed to be consistently better for the well

Included in Appendix K are statistical comparisons of the residuals for only the extraction
seasons in the test set as well as statistical comparisons of the residuals computed only on
samples with non­zero WPR in the test set. The statistical comparisons for the extraction
seasons are very similar to the ones from the full test set. However, for the statistical
comparisons of the samples with non­zero WPR, the mean model does not outperform
any of the ARIMA or LSTM models for the wells. In fact, both ARIMA and LSTM now show
significantly better performance for many of the wells in Zone 5. This is not surprising as a
large number of days with zero WPR results in a mean that is close to zero. Consequently,
the mean model is difficult to outperform for the full test set but relatively incapable when
only evaluated on non­zero WPR measurements. Comparing ARIMA and LSTM during
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
81
==PAGE:92==
Chapter 6. Results
non­zero WPR, only the ARIMA model for ST­13 has lower errors than the corresponding
LSTM model, where three LSTM models outperform ARIMA. The K`BK model continues
to be the best model for the well ST­11 whereas it is not better on the remaining wells and
zones.
The overall statistical results imply that the ARIMA and LSTM models show similar per­
formance with the LSTM models having a slight edge over the ARIMA models in periods
of water production and the reverse being true for extraction seasons and the full test set.
We now turn our attention to the wells ST­11 and ST­12 as the models were generally
most successful when applied to these wells. Here, the LSTM models displayed the
worst performance of the developed models in the statistical comparison as it was on par
with the ARIMA when evaluated on non­zero WPR samples, but had larger errors other­
wise. Hence, from the statistical analysis, it could be assumed that the K`BK is the best
model for ST­11, and the ARIMA is the best model for ST­12.However, in the statistical
comparison, only the squared residuals were considered which is only one aspect of the
forecasts.
The residuals provide information of how close the predictions are to the true observation
at every time step, but it does not give any insight into the trend of the predictions or their
uncertainty. The limitations of this metric are most easily observed from the random walk
model that simply repeats the most recent observation. This does not provide valuable
forecasts, but it has a normalised RMSE on the test set comparable to that of the K`BK
model as seen in Appendix F.1. Hence, it is also important to analyse the actual forecasts
of the models, and from these, it was observed that the only promising forecasts are made
by the LSTM models for ST­11 and ST­12. It is also worth noting that while the K`BK
model for ST­11 had a relatively low RMSE, the same was not true across the board for
these models, whereas the LSTM models were seen to have somewhat reasonable errors
across the board.
82
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:93==
7
Conclusion
In this thesis, the capabilities of three model frameworks have been investigated with the
intention of developing a model to forecast the water production of the wells at GSDs gas
storage facility in Stenlille.
First of all, several difficulties relating to the WPR data have been recognised. From our
findings, the most severe obstacle in relation to modelling the water production is that the
distribution of the water production rates seems to change over the years. This resulted in
a training, validation and test set that all seemed to reflect different distributions. Further­
more, the distributional differences of the training and validation set were more severe
when conditioned on specific groups of well. Hence, the models were probably devel­
oped on one distribution and evaluated on another. For the ARIMA and K`BK models,
it was illustrated for the well ST­17 how only one winter with sizeable water production in
the training set was enough to distort the model. The possible data set shifts influence
the LSTM networks to an even larger degree, as these are trained on one distribution,
chosen on another and evaluated on a third. It was here that the severity of the problem
was realised.
The performance of the models on the test set was generally seen to be poor. However,
modelling of the water production in wells ST­11 and ST­12 was seen to be easier com­
pared with the remaining wells. Measured on the squared prediction error, the ARIMA
and K`BK models showed better performance in comparison with the LSTM models
on the full test set. However, the forecasts from the ARIMA and K`BK models con­
sisted of smoothed repetitions of the recent past. Hence, they are not expected to deliver
satisfactory predictions for future observations. On the contrary, the forecasts from the
LSTM models for ST­11 and ST­12 managed to predict the water production on the test
set before it happened, which was visible as a mean prediction that traced the observed
water production rate. This showed that there is some predictive power present in the
data, which could probably be further exploited with proper tuning of the LSTM model.
However, some of the other wells might require an entirely different approach.
7.1
Recommendations for future work
Based on the findings in this thesis, we present some recommendations if further research
is conducted within this scope of utilising the data from the storage facility at Stenlille to
forecast the water production. Also, aspects of the data, which we believe are worth
paying attention to, are highlighted here.
7.1.1
The distribution of the WPRs appears to change over time
The greatest obstacle encountered was that the distribution of the WPRs appeared to
change over time. The distribution is known to be influenced by the temperatures in
winters as well as the operational patterns of the facility. We attempted to divide the
seasons into two subgroups, which proved insufficient. It is possible that partitioning the
data into more subgroups would mitigate the problem, but the number of subgroups is
limited by the fact that the data set only includes 12 extraction seasons.
The data set could also be divided into shorter non­overlapping sequences that could then
be split into a training and a test set using stratified sampling to make sure that each split
had members from every subgroup. Furthermore, it would be natural to only include data
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
83
==PAGE:94==
Chapter 7. Conclusion
from the extraction seasons within these short sequences. The downside of this approach
is that long­term correlations are lost when shrinking the length of the sequences.
7.1.2
Elements of the LSTM models were favourable
As the LSTM models provided somewhat reasonable forecasts for the wells ST­11 and
ST­12, a natural next step is to improve upon these models. It is also possible that this
model framework could be applied successfully to all the wells with proper tuning.
One possible improvement is to use expected production rates for the coming week in
the model. The potential benefit could be investigated by including lagged production
variables (GPR, GIR, netINJ) in the model. If the results are favourable, a coarser estimate
of the production rate could be investigated. This would have to be an estimate that could
realistically be determined by GSD one week in advance.
Another possible improvement could be to modify the loss function of the network. As
water production only occurs as a consequence of gas production, computing the loss
only on the samples with gas extraction might help the network to adapt more to the
extraction seasons, and thus, disregard periods where water production is known not to
take place.
7.1.3
The uncertainty of the WPRs if a larger resolution is used
In all models, we have used samples separated by a day. A higher resolution might pro­
vide extra information, however, the WPR observations will also be more discrete. Above
all, the problems related to the uncertainty and the uneven spacing of the WPR outlined
in Section 4.7 might also introduce new difficulties if a higher sample rate is used. It is
possible that these difficulties could be remedied through the use of a suitable encoding
of the WPR, which may only be directly applicable to a neural network.

B
Literature review
This part of the appendix provides an overview of methods and models suitable for time
series modelling that have not been employed in this thesis. Please refer to Chapter 2 for
an overview of works related to the models used in this thesis.
B.1
Exponential smoothing methods
Along with ARIMA models, exponential smoothing methods are statistical univariate mod­
els useful for forecasting on time series data.1 The common feature is that they have
exponentially decaying weights on past observations. In simple exponential smoothing
(SES), the prediction is a weighted average over the current observation and the most
recent prediction.
ˆyt+1|t = αyt + (1 ´ α)ˆyt|t´1.
It is seen that this may also be classified as an ARIMA (0, 1, 1) model. This is often stated
in the component form as it makes it easier to extend the model. These additional com­
ponents include additive and multiplicative seasonality as well as additive trend compo­
nents that may be damped. Collectively, these are sometimes referred to as ETS models,
which denotes the error, trend and seasonal components. As an example, ETS(M,A,A)
is a model with multiplicative errors, additive seasonal and trend components:
yt = (lt´1 + bt´1 + st´m) (1 + εt) ,
(B.1a)
lt = lt´1 + bt´1 + α (lt´1 + bt´1 + st´m) εt,
(B.1b)
bt = bt´1 + β (lt´1 + bt´1 + st´m) εt,
(B.1c)
st = st´m + γ (lt´1 + bt´1 + st´m) εt.
(B.1d)
The model is seen to consist of a forecast equation (yt), a level equation (lt), a trend
equation (bt) and a season equation (st). α, β, γ are all parameters between 0 and 1
which need to be specified and εt denotes one­step relative error:
εt = yt ´ (lt´1 + bt´1)
lt´1 + bt´1
The equations (B.1), which specify the ETS(M,A,A), may be divided into an observation
equation and three state or transition equations (B.1b­B.1d), which is a common feature
of the ETS models (Hyndman 2018, [31]).
ETS models are non­stationary whereas ARIMA models use differencing to model non­
stationary processes. In general, it seems that ARIMA models are the more popular of
the two though both of these are mostly used as baselines in papers to compare against
potentially more powerful methods such as neural networks (Hewamalage et al. 2021,
[28], Parmezan et al. 2019, [51], Rangapuram et al. 2018, [56], Salinas et al. 2020, [59]).
B.2
State space models
The idea behind state space models is that a given system is not directly observable.2
Therefore, the observed variables are not representative for the true underlying system
1This brief theoretical overview is based on Hyndman 2018, [31]
2The theoretical overview of all the models in Appendix B.2 is based on Särkkä 2013, [61] and Madsen
2007, [45]
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
95
==PAGE:106==
Appendix B. Literature review
(state variables). State space models can then be used to model the true underlying state
variables of a system and are characterised by two equations.
• The state equation: Xt = ft (Xt´1, ut´1, e1,t).
This equation describes the true states of the system and their dynamics. ut is a
deterministic input vector and e1,t is a random noise vector.
• The observation equation: Yt = ht (Xt, e2,t).
This equation describes the observations given the states. e2,t is a random noise
vector.
The goal of the state space models is to describe the latent states, which in principle could
be calculated as a posterior distribution of the states using Bayes’ rule:
p(Xt, Xt´1, ..., X0|Yt, Yt´1, ..., Y1).
In practice this direct calculation is computationally infeasible. Therefore, Bayesian fil­
tering is applied to the distribution of the states where the marginal posterior distribution
is:
p(Xt|Yt, Yt´1, ..., Y1),
The filtering approach is an iterative process that begins by initialising a prior distribution
of the states. From this, a prediction of the states to the next time step is made, then, then
the observations from the predicted time step are used to calculate the marginal posterior
distribution of the states.
B.2.1
Hidden Markov models
If the states Xt are discrete and only information from the current state is relevant for
predicting the next state, then the state space model is a Hidden Markov model. The
Markov property of the states can be written as:
p(Xt|Xt´1, Xt´2, ..., X0) = p(Xt|Xt´1).
(B.2)
The distribution of the observations can both be discrete and continuous, and they are
conditionally independent of the previous states and observations given the current hidden
state.
B.2.2
The Kalman filter
A state space model with the Markov property that can be expressed as
Xt = AXt´1 + But´1 + e1,t,
Yt = CXt + e2,t,
(B.3)
is a linear Gaussian system, where the noise elements are normally distributed. Fur­
thermore, the noise elements have covariance structures given by V [e1,t] = Σ1 and
V [e2,t] = Σ2. A, B and C are matrices, and if the matrices are assumed known, then the
Kalman filter can be applied to estimate the unobserved states of the linear system.
As the Kalman filter is a specific instance of the Bayesian filter, it follows the same scheme
for modelling the marginal posterior distribution of the states, where the prior distribution
of the states, x
X1|0 and Σxx
1|0, is initialised. From the initial covariance of the states, the
initial covariance of the observations is given by:
Σyy
1|0 = CΣxx
1|0CT + Σ2.

==PAGE:107==
B.2. State space models
Now the iterative procedure can begin. First, the marginal posterior distribution is esti­
mated, which is called the reconstruction step in the Kalman filter:

The Kalman filter has been applied in Stathopoulos et al. 2003, [71] for multivariate traffic
forecasting. It showed good forecasting performance, but it was only compared to an
ARIMA model, which had better forecasting performance on some streets.
B.2.3
Non­linear State Space Models
When either the state space equation or the observation equation is not linear, the Kalman
filter cannot be applied directly. The most straightforward solution is to apply a simple
transformation of the observation that linearises the system.
A similar but more computationally costly approach is the the extended Kalman filter,
which linearises the nonlinear function in a neighbourhood around the observation and
resulting state, (Särkkä 2013, [61]). These local linearisations can then be used in the
Kalman filter. Naturally, the nonlinear functions should be differential for this method to
work, but the local linearisation may still provide an unsatisfactory approximation. Fur­
thermore, the calculation of the Jacobian matrix may be computationally expensive. Other
non­linear extensions include unscented Kalman filter and particle filtering.
Wan et al. 2000, [80] compared the extended and unscented Kalman filters on a sequen­
tial data set where the unscented Kalman filter achieved the best performance without a
corresponding increase in computational complexity.
A review of particle filters, ensemble Kalman filters and Markov chain Monte Carlo is
performed in Fearnhead et al. 2018, [14] outlining the different methods and their appli­
cations, where the combination of particle filters and Markov chain Monte Carle showed
promising results.
B.2.4
EnKF­Enhanced LSTM
In this hybrid model, the output from an LSTM network is supplied as input to a Kalman
filter. The model is updated using an ensemble Kalman filter approach. The EnKF method
is explained in Aanonsen et al. 2009, [1] with a focus on the oil and gas industry. EnKF­
Enhanced LSTM has been used within the oil and gas industry to forecast production
by Loh et al. 2018, [44] and Bao et al. 2020, [7] where it was seen to produce accurate
forecasts.
Coskun et al. 2017, [11] applied an LSTM­KF model to image data where it outperformed
the both the non­hybrid LSTM and Kalman filter models.
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
97
==PAGE:108==
Appendix B. Literature review
B.2.5
SSM­RNN
Rangapuram et al. 2018, [56] used a state space model combined with a recurrent neural
network RNN to produce forecast on multivariate time series data.
B.3
Fuzzy logic
Fuzzy logic can be used to encode continuous variables as a fuzzy set. The set contains
n variables which are real numbers between 0 and 1. In relation to time series forecasting,
Fuzzy logic has primarily been used as a component in a hybrid model along with neural
networks. Singh 2017, [66] states that a fuzzy encoding of variables allows for better
utilisation in models.
Improving deep learning models with fuzzy logic was explored by Das et al. 2020, [12].
It is argued that fuzzy logic can improve deep learning models where data is noisy and
heterogeneous. Fuzzy logic may, however, increase computational complexity.
B.4
SVM
The motivation behind support Vector Machines (SVM) and other kernel methods is that
a nonlinear problem may be mapped to a feature space of potentially infinite dimension­
ality whereby the problem becomes linear. If this mapping is carefully designed, it has
a corresponding kernel and the so­called kernel trick can be used. This way an explicit
mapping is avoided and instead the problem is solved in the kernel space where all the
inner products are replaced by the appropriate kernel. In Support Vector Machine regres­
sion or Support Vector Regression (SVR), a linear regression is performed in the kernel
space. The solution to this problem is found by minimising an appropriate loss function
where the ϵ­insensitive loss is used in SVR, (Sapankevych et al. 2009, [60]).
L (y, f(x)) =

if |y ´ f(x)| ą ϵ
0,
if |y ´ f(x)| ď ϵ

minimisation problem:

yn, θT xn + θ0
*
This convex QP problem may be solved by introducing Lagrange multipliers and forming
the corresponding Lagrangian which leads to a solution that only depends on points whose
errors are larger than or equal to ϵ, i.e. the support vectors, (Theodoridis 2020, [76]).
In general, SVM methods seem to perform better than neural network methods in prob­
lems where only moderate amounts of data are available. García Nieto et al. 2018, [15]
used an SVM to forecast particulate matter where it had better performance than ARIMA
and VARMA models. Bhanja et al. 2021, [8] showed that SVM along with ARIMA are good
for univariate time series forecasting.
B.4.1
LS­SVM
The Least Squares Super Vector Machine (LS­SVM) was introduced for classification by
Suykens et al. 1999, [73]. In the regression setting, the minimisation problem is reformu­
lated as:
min

Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:109==
B.5. Gaussian process regression
This is seen to be similar to kernel ridge regression. The solution is found by solving a
linear system instead of a quadratic problem as in the original SVM, however, the solution
also lacks the sparsity of the original SVM though it may be imposed using other methods,
(Wang et al. 2005, [82]). The computational simplicity of this problem is what makes LS­
SVM attractive and it seems to be more widely used than the SVM.
LS­SVM has also been used successfully for applications within petroleum engineering
(Hanga et al. 2019, [23], Panja et al. 2018, [49], Qiao et al. 2017, [52]).
B.5
Gaussian process regression
Gaussian process regression (GPR) is a non­parametric method where these kernels are
used to incorporate priors into the regression task and it falls within the family of kernel
methods.3 Broadly speaking, GPR models a set of probability distributions over possible
functions that all fit a set of training points. This probabilistic viewpoint naturally gives rise
to confidence intervals within the regression setting.
A Gaussian process is defined by a mean and a covariance function.
f(x) „ GP
)
µ(x), K(x, x1)
*
Depending on the choice of kernel, different sets of hyperparameters should be tuned.
Among others, these hyperparameters control smoothing. The real­valued kernel matrix
specifies the covariance, i.e. the uncertainty for every x. This uncertainty increases away
from the fitting points while it is small in the neighbourhoods surrounding the observa­
tions. Bayesian inference may then be used to take into account both the priors and the
observations.
In the regression task, it is assumed the target function follows a multivariate normal dis­
tribution with a kernel as covariance matrix. This assumption simplifies the update task
as the sum of independent Gaussian random variables is always Gaussian.
As an example, modelling the additive noise process: y(x) = f(x) + ϵ with ϵ being zero
mean with variance β´1. Under the assumption that the inputs and the noise are inde­
pendent, the covarince matrix of the targets is the sum of the covariances: C = K + β´1I.
The predictive distribution of the targets given the training data (ytr) is then completely
specified by the mean and covariance matrix.
p (ytr, yte) = N
+1µtr
µte
2
,
1Ctr, Ctr,te
Cte,tr, Cte
2,
p(yte|ytr) = N
)
µte + Cte,trC´1
tr (ytr ´ µtr) , Cte ´ Cte,trC´1
tr Ctr,te
*
It is seen that prediction requires inverse covariance of training data which may be expen­
sive to compute for large amounts of data. For this reason, does not scale well with data
though there are approximate alternatives. Krauth et al. 2017, [36] also cites this lack of
scalability as one of the reasons why much current research focuses on neural networks
instead of kernel methods and points out the overuse of the radial basis function kernel
impairs the performance of kernel methods in general.
Depending on the specific choice of kernel, there are a number of hyperparameters to
be determined which may be determined using numerical optimisation methods. As with
other kernel methods, the performance of the method is also somewhat sensitive to the
choice of kernel.
3This brief overview is based on Theodoridis 2020, [76] and Bishop 2006, [9]
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
99
==PAGE:110==
Appendix B. Literature review
In one of the few recent surveys that included GPR, Ahmed et al. 2010, [3] compares it
favourably to a number of machine learning methods for univariate time series forecasting.
It is, however, worth noticing that most of the implementations were very basic. In general,
GPR does not seem to be a popular method for time series forecasting.
B.6
Intrinsic mode functions
A time series can be decomposed into intrinsic mode functions (IMF) using Empirical
mode decomposition (EEMD) or the Hilbert­Huang transform. These IMFs represent os­
cillatory modes and may be viewed as a general counterpart to harmonic functions. They
may be constructed in a recursive manner from the data with a stopping criterion that
depends on the method. This adaptive nature makes it applicable to nonlinear and non­
stationary processes since the method only depends on local characteristics. The repre­
sentation can be viewed as a generalised Fourier expansion and can be presented as as
a Hilbert Spectrum, (Huang et al. 2005, [30]).
The method has been used in oil production forecasting by Liu et al. 2020, [41] where
EEMD was used to compute IMFs. Three stable IMFs were identified fed into an LSTM­
network which provided good forecasts compared with an SVM using an RBF kernel and
a reference ANN.

In this part of the appendix, it is shown, how the expression used to describe the break­
through time in Section 3.1.1 is derived from the original equations from The Bournazel­
Jeanson Method.
C.1
The Bournazel­Jeanson Method
The Bournazel­Jeanson Method (Ahmed 2019, [4]) for calculating the breakthrough time
of water is given by:
tBT = 20 325
µo h φ t(D,BT)
(ρw ´ ρo) kv (1 + Mα).
(C.1)
From this equation the breakthrough time of water tBT is observed to be positively cor­
related with the viscosity of oil µo, the oil column thickness h, the porosity φ and the
dimensionless breakthrough time of water t(D,BT). It is observed to be negatively corre­
lated with the water­oil density difference (ρw ´ ρo), the vertical permeability kv and the
water­oil mobility ratio M, as the exponent α = 0.5 when 0 ď M ď 1 and α = 0.6 when
1 ă M ď 10. The dimensionless breakthrough time of water is given by:
t(D,BT) =

that when Z Ñ

break through to the well openings when z ě

.
(C.3)
From this equation, Z is observed to be positively correlated with the water­oil density
difference (ρw ´ ρo), the horizontal permeability kh and the oil column thickness h. Z is
observed to be negatively correlated with the distance from the top of the reservoir to the
bottom of the perforations hp, the viscosity of oil µo, the oil production rate Qo and the oil
formation volume factor Bo. Multiplying the formation oil volume factor to the volume of oil
at reservoir conditions produces the volume of oil at ground level conditions. Bo is likely
numerically greater for gas extraction.
From the equations above, tBT is known to be positively correlated with t(D,BT) and thus
also Z. However, Equation (C.1) shows that tBT has positive correlation with µo and neg­
ative correlation with (ρw´ρo). This is the opposite of Z, which from Equation (C.3) is seen
to be negatively correlated with µo and positively correlation with (ρw ´ ρo). Therefore,
Equation (C.2) and (C.3) are inserted in Equation (C.1) to better examine the correlations
between tBT and the variables µo and (ρw ´ ρo). This results in the following equation for

D
Spectrogram
In this part of the appendix, an example of a spectrogram applied to the WPR of the well
ST­11 is shown.
D.1
Spectrogram of ST­11
In Figure D.1, a spectrogram of the WPR for ST­11 is included along with the time series
for the same period. The measured WPRs in the bottom figure are observed to resemble
a series of positive impulses. The Fourier transformation does not apply well to discon­
tinuous signals as discussed in Section 3.4, as impulses will return a constant value at
all frequencies. This is easily observed for the WPR measurement occurring around 21
January 2019. Here, the spectrum is observed to represent all frequencies at a constant
value. Hence, the frequency domain does not seem to supply a simpler representation of
the WPR, and these impulses might also be difficult for models to handle.
Figure D.1: The top figure shows the spectrogram of the water production for the well
ST­11 during the winter of 2019. The bottom figure shows the corresponding WPR mea­
surements.

E
Data and data cleaning
This part of the appendix provides additional details related to the data set and the data
cleaning process described in Chapter 4.
E.1
Length of the time series
The earliest measurements in the data set are from 1 October 2009, however, a few
of the wells were either moved or taken into production after this date. The wells that
were moved from Zone 1­3 to Zone 5 are ST7, ST13 and ST17 which were moved on
12 November 2009, 13 November 2009 and 26 March 2010 respectively. The well ST20
was taken into production on 17 November 2009, (Stade et al. 2020, [70]). For the wells
that were moved, earlier data was discarded and the cumulative variables were reset.
Furthermore, a manual inspection of the data revealed that the days immediately after
production was initiated contained erroneous values due to tests. Therefore, these days
were also removed from the beginning of the respective time series.
E.2
Unused variables
The following section is a short description of the measured and derived variables from
the wells that were not included in the models developed in this thesis.
E.2.1
Sand production rate (SPR)
When gas is extracted from the reservoirs, sand is withdrawn as a byproduct like water.
The sand is extracted in very small amounts, and it does not affect the operation of the
wells to the degree water production does. Part of the sand accumulates at the bottom
of the pipes below the perforations. The sand production is not directly measured, but is
calculated using acoustic measurements. Among others, the measurements are affected
by raindrops hitting the pipe. SPR is therefore considered as having a large uncertainty.
As SPR does not significantly affect the gas plant operation, and the measurements hereof
are very uncertain, it has been omitted from the analysis in this thesis.
E.2.2
Annulus A pressure (ANP)
ANP is the measured pressure in the inner casings that surrounds the production pipe.
ANP is used to detect leaks from the production pipe, which results in an alarm. When
there are no leaks, the ANP varies with the changing temperature, which is affected by
the temperature of the gas in the production pipe. For this reason, the variable is not
expected to contain useful information about the production beyond what is captured by
THT. Therefore, the ANP has been omitted from the analysis in this thesis.
E.2.3
Casing (annulus B) pressure (CASP)
CASP is the measured pressure in the void between the outer and inner casing that sur­
rounds the production pipe. Therefore, CASP is a security measurement behaving like
ANP and has been omitted from the analysis in this thesis.
E.2.4
Cumulative variables (cumGIR, cumGPR, cumWPR)
These cumulative variables are calculated directly from GIR, GPR and WPR. Further­
more, the information from cumGIR and cumGPR is contained in netINJ albeit with equal
weight. When the ARIMA and K`BK models were developed, it became apparent that
the cumulative variables were non­stationary and would at least need first order differenc­
ing, which would transform them to GIR, GPR and WPR. Therefore, these variables were
omitted from the analysis in this thesis.
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
105
==PAGE:116==
Appendix E. Data and data cleaning
E.2.5
Water production index (WPi)
The WPi is a binary variable that is 1 when the WPR is non­zero. As this variable contains
no extra information regarding WPR and the amount of water production is of interest, WPi
has been omitted from the analysis in this thesis.
E.2.6
Water gas ratio (WGR)
WGR is computed as WPR/ (GPR ¨ 1000). WGR does not contain additional information
on the water production as it is calculated directly from WPR and GPR. Moreover, this
variable is not well­defined when GPR is zero. Therefore, WGR has been omitted from
the analysis in this thesis.
E.3
Algorithms for data cleaning
This section provides the algorithms used for cleaning the variables WPR, THP and THT.
For each of the algorithms for WPR and THP, there is a plot showing a specific result of
the cleaning applied to a well.

`2im`M
A specific result of the THP cleaning algorithm applied to the well ST­7 is illustrated in
Figure E.3 where the raw and cleaned THP are plotted alongside the GIR and the GPR.
For this particular well, none of the raw THP measurements exceeds 150 bara. However,
many of the THP values are below the lower thresholds specified by condition 1 and 2.
Figure E.3: Raw and cleaned THP for ST­7 plotted alongside the GPR and the GIR.
E.3.3
Algorithm for THT data cleaning
The following is the implementation of the algorithm in Python used for cleaning the tub­
ing head temperature data for the wells. In addition to the erroneous values found by the
algorithm, 4 periods of extra faulty measurements were identified for 3 wells and the in­
dices of these periods were manually inputted to the algorithm that cleaned these values
as well. These indices are listed in Table E.4

Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:121==
F
Random walk model results
In this part of the appendix, the results for the random walk model are discussed. The
normalised RMSEs are listed for the 7­day predictions and the raw RMSEs are listed for all
prediction horizons up to and including seven for each well are included in Appendix F.2.
F.1
Normalised RMSE results for random walk model
The simple random walk model is applied to the WPR of all the wells and zones. These
results show that the random walk model generally performs better on very short­term
forecast. However, we are interested in a model capable of forecasting a week of water
production. Therefore, we primarily analyse the RMSEs for the 7­day forecasts.
In Table F.1, the normalised RMSE for each zone’s and well’s 7­day forecasts are shown.
In the table, it is seen that on the whole training data, the random walk model only has a
lower RMSE than the mean model for the 3 wells ST­11, ST­17 and ST­19. When focusing
on the extraction season for the training data, the random walk model is seen to have a
lower RMSE than the mean model for 5 wells and Zone 5.
Table F.1: The table shows the normalised RMSE for the random walk model using a
7­day prediction horizon. The raw RMSEs for all prediction horizons up to 7 are listed in
Appendix F.2.

The random walk model has a lower RMSE on the wells with more continuous periods of
water water production. This is especially apparent for ST­11, where it is seen to consis­
tently have better performance than the mean model. On the test set, the random walk
model performs best on ST­9, but this well is not used regularly, therefore, the analysis
hereof is of little importance. ST­11 and ST­12 are the only two other wells where the
random walk model has a lower RMSE than the mean model. These also seem to be the
wells that have the most consistent water production over the extraction season. How­
ever, the RMSEs for the 7­day forecasts of the random walk model are in general not
better than the mean model.

G
ARIMA
In this part of the appendix, additional documentation related to the ARIMA models is
included. This covers an example of model development using the Box­Jenkins method,
the ARIMA coefficients, log­likelihood plots and the RMSEs.
G.1
Development of an ARIMA model for the water
production in ST­17
As an example illustrating the Box­Jenkins method, a model for WPR in ST­17 is built. ST­
17 is chosen for this example because this well produces a substantial amount of water
and has water production for an extended period of time every extraction season. The
logarithm of WPR for ST­17 is plotted in Figure G.1 where the training data is shown in
blue and the test data is shown in red. In the figure, a considerable water production is
seen in the period from 2017­03­14 to 2017­05­16. The 5­minute data for this period has
been investigated and a steady water production throughout these days was observed.
This caused the high daily mean WPR values in the period. We consulted GSD who
confirmed that these WPR measurements were not abnormal.
Figure G.1: Logarithm of daily mean of WPR for ST­17. The value 0.001 was added to
the daily mean of WPR to avoid zero values.
Only, the training set was used to develop the ARIMA model. First, the ACF and PACF of
WPR were investigated. These are shown in Figure G.2 until lag 400 to also search for
yearly seasonal dependencies in the data. Even though a period with water production
is present every winter, no yearly dependency in either the ACF or PACF is visible. This
is probably due to the extraction period not occurring on the same dates every winter.
Among others, extraction is driven by the increased demand in winter due to low temper­
atures. However, these periods with low temperatures differ in both duration and time of
occurrence throughout the years.
Orders of the AR and MA parts for ST­17
In Figure G.2, it is seen that the ACF drops fast towards zero, which indicate stationarity
and no differencing is needed. There is a large value in lag 1 for the PACF while the
ACF looks like an exponential decay so an AR(1) term was added to the ARIMA model in
accordance with Table 3.3.
An ARIMA(1,0,0) model is then fitted with the `BK­function from the package 7Q`2+bi
in _, and the ACF and PACF of the residuals are shown in Figure G.3 until lag 100 as

Figure G.5: Residuals of the ARIMA(1,0,1) model on WPR for ST­17.
The residuals of the ARIMA(1,0,1) are shown in Figure G.5. Here, it is seen that the
largest residuals are present in the period from 2017­03­14 to 2017­05­16 at the end of
the training data, where the water production was seen to be large in Figure G.1. These
residuals are present in two periods, which are separated by a bit more than a month.
Hence, it is possible that fitting a coefficient at lag 37 might only benefit this specific period.
To investigate if this is the case, an ARIMA(1,0,1) is fitted to the training data excluding
this last period with the large water production. The ACF and PACF of the residuals of
this model are shown in Figure G.6.
In Figure G.6, it is seen that the significant correlation at lag 37 has disappeared com­
pletely. Therefore, it would be inappropriate to add a seasonal coefficient at lag 37 as
this would simply tune the ARIMA model to the high water production rates that are only

G.1. Development of an ARIMA model for the water production in ST­17
Box­Cox transformation for ST­17
As mentioned in Section 3.2.3, transformations of the data were also considered. This
was done using a range­mean plot to detect non­stationary variance. Hence, the range
and the mean of each year are plotted in Figure G.8. The top plot includes all 7 available
calendar years from 2010 to the beginning of the test set in May 2017. The bottom plot
excludes the last year of the training set due to the large values of the WPR. If there is a
strong relationship in the range­mean plot, i.e. if the variability increases with the level, it
may indicate the need for a transformation and the suggested transformation is called a
Box­Cox transformation (Madsen 2007, [45]):
Y ˚
t =
#
Y λ´1
λ
,
λ ‰ 0
ln Yt,
λ = 0
Figure G.8: Range mean plot of ST­17. Top figure includes all 7 years in training set.
Bottom figure includes the first 6 years of training set.
The relationship in the top plot in Figure G.8 may be indicative of using λ = 0. However,
in the bottom plot, which excludes the last year of the training set, the trend may indicate
λ ą 0 or no transformation at all as there is no strong relationship present. Even though
Figure G.8 did not provide strong support for using a transformation of the WPR, we tried to
investigate if the transformed data lead to a more adequate model. The "Qt*QtXHK#/­
function in _ is used to find a suitable λ for the transformation, and the function outputs
λ = 0.16, which is in the neighbourhood of the values suggested by the range mean plots.
Residual analysis of the original and transformed ARIMA model
The Box­Cox transformation is applied to the training data and the model building of the
ARIMA model is performed in the same matter as described above. The resulting model
is an ARIMA(1,1,2) model. As the input of this model is a transformation of the data, the
log likelihood cannot be compared directly to the log likelihood of the ARIMA(1,0,1) in the
original scale. Therefore, a residual analysis is performed to investigate, which of the
models output residuals that resemble white noise the most.
In Figure G.9 the histograms and the QQ­plots of the residuals from the two models are
shown. Here it is seen that both models have a lot of residuals very close to zero. How­

Lastly, a sign test was performed for the residuals. For a white noise process, there
is 50% chance of a sign change from εt to εt+1. From this, a 95% confidence interval
for the number of sign changes can be calculated for the residuals. In Table G.10, this
confidence interval is compared to the actual number of sign changes for the two models.
This also shows that the residuals from the original ARIMA(1,0,1) resemble white noise
better than those of the transformed model. As there is no improvement from transforming
the input, it is concluded that the best ARIMA model for describing the WPR of ST­17 is
the ARIMA(1,0,1) with the data in the original scale. The final model is estimated with the
parameters shown in Table G.11.
Even though the ARIMA(1,0,1) model was the best of the investigated ARIMA models for
describing the water production data from ST­17, the results from this section demonstrate
that the residuals of the model do not resemble white noise sufficiently. The Ljung­box

statistic in Figure G.7 showed that the residuals of the model are correlated at lags greater
than 4. Furthermore, the sign test also showed that the number of sign changes lies well
outside the 95% confidence interval for a white noise process. These problems are likely
because only 699 of the 2611 days in the training set have non­zero WPR, which means
the water production equals zero around 73% of the days in the training set and this
structure is hard to describe for an ARIMA model.

Box­Jenkins methodology, every model order up to AR­10 and MA­10 have been tried on
data for the water production rate. The resulting likelihoods for the optimal parameters are
shown in the following figures. Please note that the scale has been changed in many of the
plots due to a low minimum value which would otherwise have distorted the scale heavily.
These very small likelihoods are coloured red and their values have been annotated in
the caption. Additionally, a ”1” is used to indicate an ARIMA model where at least one of
the AR parameters are greater than 1.0.

H.2. K`BK coefficients
H.2
K`BK coefficients
The coefficients of the K`BK models are shown in this section as a series of heat maps.
Please note that these models have only been developed for Zone 1­3, Zone 5, ST­11 and
ST­17. The coefficients belonging to the autoregressive part are in the top four rows while
MA coefficients are in the bottom four rows. A white square indicates that the coefficient
is zero.

H.3

The RMSE for the K`BK models are listed in this section. In each table, the errors are

for the mean model with the mean computed on the training set. The RMSEs have been
computed on six different subsets of the data. These are the full training and test sets,
their extractions seasons as well as the days with non­zero water production.
