I
Synthetic well data and testing of LSTM
model implementation
In this part of the appendix, a description of how the synthetic well data set was created is
provided. The synthetic data set was created with the purpose of testing the implementa­
tion of the LSTM model before it was applied to the real well data. This process is outlined
in Appendix I.2.
I.1
Synthetic well data
The synthetic data was created with a resolution of one day, which is the same sample
rate used for the real data. The synthetic data also consists of the same variables used
from the real data set in the LSTM model. The synthetic data was based on the well ST­
11 meaning that its netINJ and the netINJ from Zone 1­3 was used directly. The reason
being that this well was seen to be the easiest one to model using the ARIMA and K`BK
models. The results from the ARIMA and K`BK models where generated before the
implementation of the LSTM model and they are discussed in Section 6.1 and Section 6.2.
The variables in synthetic well data are listed Table I.1 with the column ”Source” indicating
whether the variable was synthetically generated or taken from the real data.
Table I.1: Variables in the synthetic data set.
Variable
Source
Zone netINJ
Zone 1­3
nejINJ
ST­11
GPR
ST­11
GIR
ST­11
WPR
Synthetic
THP
Synthetic
THT
Synthetic
As GPR and GIR are controlled directly by GSD, these variables along with the net in­
jections are taken directly from ST­11 and Zone 1­3. We expected that there could be
valuable information in the netINJ for the Zone 1­3, which the well is perforated to, be­
cause this will give insight into the total amount of gas in the zone.
From these variable, we obtain the unevenly spaced seasonal variations in the data, which
was found difficult to model by the ARIMA and K`BK models. The variables from ST­11
was then used to generate the synthetic data for the variables WPR, THP and THT. This
ensures that the synthetic variables are correlated with the original variables.
I.1.1
WPR
From Chapter 4, it is known that there can only be water production, when there is gas
production. Therefore, a requirement for WPR > 0 is GPR > 0. Water production is also
more prone to happen when the amount of working gas in the reservoir is low. Therefore,
it is assumed that water production cannot occur, when the reservoir is around maximum
capacity, so another condition for WPR is netINJ < 0. Lastly, water production is more
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
143
==PAGE:154==
Appendix I. Synthetic well data and testing of LSTM model implementation
prone to happen if the gas production was large in the recent past. GPR for ST­11 is in
the range from Nm3/h to 38 000 Nm3/h, and it is chosen that the 7­day average of GPR
has to be above 5 000 Nm3/h leading up to a measurement of WPR > 0. For samples that
met these three conditions, the synthetic WPR was sampled from the following process:
WPRt =
+
´netINJt
50
,2
ˆ GPRt
40
ˆ GPR(7 day mean)
t
15
+ εt,
where εt is white noise with variance σ2 = 0.12. The denominators were chosen based
on the maximum absolute values of netINJ, GPR and the mean of the 7­day GPR. The
quadratic term and the standard deviation were chosen such that the synthetic WPR vi­
sually resembled the original WPR. The WPR for the synthetic data is shown in Figure I.2
along with the data from the well ST­11.
Figure I.2: The figure on the top shows the WPR and the THP for the synthetic data, while
the bottom plot contains the WPR and the THP for the well ST­11 whose gas rates were
used as a starting point for the synthetic data.
I.1.2
THP
The yearly variations in THP are very similar to those of netINJ as they both describe the
amount of gas in the reservoir. THP is known to experience greater fluctuations in periods
144
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
==PAGE:155==
I.1. Synthetic well data
with either gas production or injection. The THP also experiences drops in its value when
water is produced. Hence, THP was sampled from the following process:
THPt = (netINJt + 140) ´ 5 WPRt + GIRt ˆ ε(1,t) + GPRt ˆ ε(1,t) + ε(2,t)
where ε(1,t) and ε(2,t) are white noise with the variances σ2
1 = 0.22 and σ2
2 = 0.052 re­
spectively. the value 140 is added to netINJ to make the scale more similar to THP. The
other values are found by experimentation to mimic THP. The synthetic THP is shown in
Figure I.2. It is seen that the synthetic THP has a slightly different scale compared with
the real THP from ST­11. Also, the peaks and troughs vary more across the years for the
synthetic data, which is a direct consequence of generating the pressure from the gas
rates. These dissimilarities could be adjusted, but this has not been done as the purpose
of the synthetic data is only to test the implementation of the LSTM model.
I.1.3
THT
THT follows yearly and daily temperature variations in the absence of gas production
and injection. In periods of gas injection, THT is observed to be around 33 °C, and the
temperature is around 29 °C when there is gas production.
Figure I.3: The figure on the top shows the THT for the synthetic data while the bottom
plots contains the THT for the well ST­11 whose gas rates were used as a starting point
for the synthetic data
THT was generated from a sine curve to mimic the yearly seasonal pattern. The date of
temperature minimum was set to the end of January, and similarly, the maximum tem­
perature was set to occur at the end of July. The sine curve varies from 1 °C in winter to
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning
145
==PAGE:156==
Appendix I. Synthetic well data and testing of LSTM model implementation
18 °C in summer. As daily changes occur in the temperature, white noise with standard
deviation of 0.5 was added to this curve. This process was used to draw a sample for
THT in the absence of gas flow, i.e. GPR == 0 and GIR == 0. However, during periods
of gas injection or production THT was sampled from the processes:
THTt = 30 + GIRt
5
+ εt
,
GIRt ą 0,
THTt = 26 + GPRt
5
+ εt
,
GPRt ą 0,
where εt is white noise with σ2 = 0.22. GIR and GPR were divided by 5 to make THT
average around 33 °C when GIR ą 0 and around 29 °C when GPR ą 0. The synthetic
THT is shown in Figure I.3.
I.2
The LSTM model applied to synthetic data
The LSTM model for the synthetic data was developed following the methods described
in more detail in Section 5.4, where sequences of 2 years were used as input to forecast
7 days of WPR. A grid search was performed to find the best set of hyperparameters
(number of hidden units, number of layers, dropout rate in the fully connected layer and
LSTM dropout rate) for the LSTM model. Each combination of hyperparameters was
trained three times with the Adam optimiser with a learning rate of 0.001 for 200 epochs.
The mean of the minimum validation MSE for each run can be seen in Figure Figure I.4
where each tile corresponds to a different set of hyperparameters. The numbers reported
in the heat map are the mean of the minimum validation MSE for each set of hyperpa­
rameters ˆ105. From this figure, the set of hyperparameters that minimises the mean
validation MSE is seen to be 30 hidden units, 1 layer, 0.5 for the dropout rate in fully
connected layer and no LSTM dropout. Ideally, more than three runs for each set of hy­
perparameter should have been trained. This would allow us to compare the distributions
of the minimum validation MSEs and to test for statistically significant differences. Due to
the computational time, only three runs were made and a simple selection based on the
minimum mean of validation MSE was used. Also, as the purpose of this exercise was
only to test the implementation, determining the hyperparameters precisely is not deemed
important.
For the optimal set of hyperparameters, the learning curves for the training and validation
loss can be seen in Figure I.5. For this run, the minimum validation MSE of 0.00168 occurs
at epoch 76, which is marked by the vertical, green line. From this figure, it is observed
that the LSTM model succeeds in decreasing the loss on the training data. Furthermore,
the validation loss is observed to decrease until epoch 76 where starts to increase. This
shows that the aspects learned after this epoch probably do not generalise to the validation
set, meaning that the model probably starts to fit to the noise in the training data, which is
an example of overfitting. Hence, the model that generalises the best to the validation set
is the one with the weight estimates from epoch 76. It is worth noting that the validation
loss is lower than the training loss, which is most likely the result of differences between
the training and validation sets.
To investigate if the model actually fits to the synthetic well data, the 1­day and 7­day
forecasts are shown for a winter in the training set in Figure I.6. In the leftmost figure
for the 1­day forecasts, it is observed that the model succeeds in describing the overall
trend in the training data. Furthermore, the 95% prediction intervals are seen to be snug
in the absence of water production while they expand during extraction. However, the
146
Optimisation of Gas Plant Operation using Machine Learning

the same time index, only correlations at the same time step are ensured. Hence, only
nowcasting is guaranteed unless netINJ or GPR can be forecasted by themselves. This
could be remedied by shifting the time series relative to each other. However, the results
from the 1­day forecasts imply that the LSTM model can indeed capture some of the
interdependencies describing the underlying process of the WPR at least for this well,
which was the purpose of applying it to the synthetic data first. Therefore, we proceeded
with this LSTM implementation on the real well data.

the rightmost figure shows the 7­day forecasts.

J
LSTM
This part of the appendix supplies documentation regarding the LSTM models. This in­
cludes an outline of the architecture, learning curves for the wells and the RMSEs.
J.1
LSTM network architectures in related studies
Loh et al. 2018, [44] implemented a network with 6 vertically stacked LSTM blocks and
two fully connected layers at the end to predict the gas production from mature wells. The
task of the two fully connected layers was to transform the output of the last LSTM block
to the dimension of the target. They used sequences of 36 time steps to forecast 1 time
step following each sequence. Each input sequence was shifted by one time step relative
to the previous sequence.
Bao et al. 2020, [7] forecasted the oil production and water cut from oil reservoirs. A matrix
was used to map the output of the LSTM block to the shape of the target variable. They
tune the number of vertical layers as well as applying input sequences of 49 time steps
to the network, which then predicts the following time step.
Liu et al. 2020, [41] used a network based on the LSTM block to forecast production from
oil fields. The network consisted of three vertically stacked LSTM blocks and the Adam
optimiser was used to tune the weights.
Karevan et al. 2020, [34] implemented an LSTM model for weather forecasting. They
applied sliding sequences to forecast the time steps following the input sequence. They
used an LSTM block for the input sequence and a fully connected layer at the end to
transform the output to the target size.
J.2
Network implementation
The following is the implementation of the network in PyTorch using Python.

J.6
Normalised RMSEs for the field model
Table J.30 shows the normalised RMSE for the LSTM model trained on data from all the
wells. This table is similar to Table 6.10 in Section 6.3 where the normalised errors were
listed for the models on the individual wells. A comparison between these two tables
shows that training error generally decreases when the field model is trained on the in­
dividual well, which was also seen for the losses in Section 5.4.7. Notable exceptions
to this trend are ST­18 and ST­19 where the error on the training set actually increases,
which is presumably a result of the data set shift commented on in Section 4.8.1.

==PAGE:171==
K
Statistical comparison
In this part of the appendix, the statistical comparisons of the residuals from the models
are shown. These residuals are computed on the extraction seasons in the test set as
well as on the samples with non­zero WPR in the test set. Comparisons of the residuals
on the full test set are included in Section 6.4.
Statistical comparisons for the extraction seasons in the test set
From the 57 pairwise comparisons for the extraction seasons, there are 33 which yield a
significant difference in the performance of the compared models using a p­value thresh­
old of 0.05. The pFDR for these 33 rejected null hypotheses is 0.000787 which means
that 0.026 is the number of falsely rejected null hypotheses.

Appendix K. Statistical comparison
Statistical comparisons for samples in the test set with non­zero WPR
From the 57 pairwise comparisons for non­zero WPR, there are 33 which yield a significant
difference in the performance of the compared models using a p­value threshold of 0.05.
The pFDR for these 33 rejected null hypotheses is 0.000422 which means that 0.014 is
the number of falsely rejected null hypotheses.
